

2007 WL 1168515 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALVIN DANIEL MANSILLA-PALENCIA
File: AXX XX4 261 - Hartford
March 2, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hamza Ma’ayergi, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 28, 2006, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on September 22, 1998. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent failed to appear for his hearing on September 22, 1998, and was ordered removed in absentia. In his motion to reopen, the respondent acknowledges that he departed the United States on November 26, 1999, to visit his mother in Guatemala. Furthermore, he re-entered the United States on March 10, 2002, using another individual’s passport. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, at 2. In a decision dated November 28, 2006, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s departure from the United States in November 1999, was a self-deportation under 8 C.F.R. § 1241.7. Therefore, there are no proceedings to reopen.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge should have exercised his discretion and reopened the proceedings because the respondent established that he failed to appear for his hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. On the notice of appeal, the respondent asserts that the proceedings should be reopened pursuant to Contreras-Rodriguez v. U.S. Attorney Gen., 462 F.3d 1314 (11th Cir. 2006)(court determined that Immigration Judges and the Board retain jurisdiction to reopen proceedings to address whether an alien received sufficient notice of a removal hearing, subsequent to an alien’s removal from the United States). The respondent also asserts that 8 C.F.R. § 1241.7. violates the respondent’s right to due process. However, it is well settled that this Board lacks jurisdiction to rule upon the constitutionality of the Act. See section 103 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1103; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1; Liu v. Waters, 55 F.3d 421 (9th Cir. 1995); Matter of Fuentes-Campos, 21 I&N Dec. 905 (BIA 1997); Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, at 532 (BIA 1992).
 
We find that the Board and the Immigration Judge lack jurisdiction over a motion to reopen after the respondent’s departure from the United States pursuant to a final order of removal. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998). “A motion to reopen or reconsider shall not be made by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.” 8 C.F.R.§§ 1003.2(d), 1003.23(b); see also Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864 (BIA 1994) (finding that once the Board’s order of deportation was legally, executed, the proceedings were brought to finality, and the Board lacks jurisdiction to act on a motion to reopen). The respondent avers that his proceedings should be reopened pursuant to Contreras-Rodriguez v. U.S. Attorney Gen., supra. However, the case cited by the respondent was issued in the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals. Consequently, it is inapplicable in the instant proceedings and it has no precedential value. Moreover, the respondent knew that his hearing was scheduled for September 22, 1998. See Exh. 2, Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, at 1. The respondent has not presented any pertinent legal authority to support an assertion that the Immigration Judge and the Board retain jurisdiction to consider a motion to reopen subsequent to an alien’s removal from the United States.1 The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s departure from the United States in November 1999, was a self-deportation under 8 C.F.R. § 1241.7. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In light of our finding that the Board and the Immigration Judge lack jurisdiction over a motion to reopen aider the respondent’s departure from the United States pursuant to a final order of removal, we decline to address the remaining issues raised on appeal.

2007 WL 1168658 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOUNG HAE CHUNG
File: AXX XX8 147 - Los Angeles
March 1, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sunmin Lee, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals the decision of an Immigration Judge entered on November 16, 2005, decision. The appeal, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded, will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a citizen of the South Korea, was charged with being present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. At a November 16, 2005, hearing, counsel indicated that the respondent desired adjustment of status based on her marriage to a United States citizen. The respondent’s motion for administrative closure was denied. At the November 16, 2005, hearing, current counsel sought only voluntary departure under 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a) (Tr. at 10). After consultation with the respondent, off the record, counsel confirmed that pre-conclusion voluntary departure was being sought and waived appeal (Tr. at 11-14). See Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000)(grant of voluntary departure prior to completion of removal proceeding, under Section 240B(a) of the Act, requires an express waiver of the right to appeal); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i).
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal).
 
The respondent suggests that her waiver was invalid. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). She argues that she received ineffective assistance from her former counsel, that she was told to just say “yes” to everything, and that she did not know that she was waiving the right to appeal or agreeing to depart voluntarily. However, in this case, we note that the respondent was not forced to seek pre-hearing voluntary departure, which requires an appeal waiver. Rather, counsel sought such relief on her behalf, and waived the right to appeal (Tr. at 11-14). The respondent fails to show that she was coerced into accepting pre-hearing voluntary departure or was inhibited from seeking any viable form of relief from removal. She has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, supra, requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he or she was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). The respondent has provided no affidavit setting forth the agreement with her former counsel, has not shown that her former attorney has been given the opportunity to respond to the allegations, and has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority.
 
*2 We therefore lack jurisdiction over this case. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1168658 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 927159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUSTINA AFEBANYE AFEBENDE A.K.A. JUSTINA AFEBANYE ADIE A.K.A. JUSTINA A. AFEBENDE A.K.A. JUSTINA AFEBANYE ADIE AFEBENDE
File: AXX XX8 327 - Arlington
February 28, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eleanor J. Tschugunov, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Victoria A. Christian
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N ACt ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; voluntary departure; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. At her hearing before the Immigration Judge on March 16, 2001, the respondent waived her right to appeal and accepted a period of pre-conclusionary voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a) (Tr. at 12-13). We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(2)(G). However, even assuming arguendo that we have jurisdiction in this case, the respondent is ineligible for relief from removal because she remained in the United States beyond the date authorized for voluntary departure after receiving oral and written warnings of the consequences of her failure to depart from both the Immigration Judge and this Board (I.J. at 10). See section 240B(d)(1)(B) of the Act. Contrary to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, the Board lacks authority to apply an “exceptional circumstances” or other general equitable exception to the penalty provisions for her failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure. See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87 (BIA 2007). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record for further proceedings based upon her claim that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent’s motion does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Among other things, Matter of Lozada requires that an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel file a complaint against the allegedly-ineffective attorney or provide an adequate explanation for failing to file such a complaint. In her motion, the respondent notes that she has not filed a bar complaint against her former attorney because “this case does not involve failure on the part of the prior counsel with respect to ethical or legal responsibilities; rather the complaint goes to prior counsel’s failure to timely notify the ¿Boardº of an additional basis for relief” (Motion to Remand at 3). For the reasons stated in Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996), we find the respondent’s explanation for her failure to file a bar complaint against her former counsel to be inadequate. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
  
Roger A. Pauley
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2007 WL 927159 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 1123220 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERNELLA JOY FORRESTER
File: AXX XX2 960 - Key West, Florida
February 26, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa M. Okoh, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This matter was last before us on October 3, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’ s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s May 22, 2006, decision, finding the respondent removable for commission of an aggravated felony, as defined by section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B); denying her application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), as a matter of discretion; and ordering her removal to Jamaica, for lack of jurisdiction.1 The Board noted, however, that the respondent’s brief alleged that she received ineffective assistance of counsel by former counsel concerning the denial of relief under section 212(c) of the Act. We construed those statements to be a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Since we lacked jurisdiction to address the respondent’s appeal, we also lacked jurisdiction to address a motion to reopen. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); see also Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). Therefore we returned the record to the Immigration Court for the Immigration Judge to consider and adjudicate the respondent’s motion to reopen. In a decision dated October 25, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel as to his presentation of her application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, noting that the record reflected that former counsel had presented “all the evidence and witnesses” and the respondent had been afforded a “fair hearing.” The respondent’s timely appeal of that decision will be dismissed.
 
At the outset, we consider the respondent’s appellate contentions that the Immigration Judge failed to comply with the Board’s remand order, i.e., to consider and address her arguments as to validity of her waived appeal, to be an inaccurate characterization of our decision. The Board’s October 3, 2006, decision specifically noted that in the brief submitted in support of the respondent’s appeal on August 29, 2006, the respondent’s present counsel contested the Immigration Judge’s May 22, 2006, decision, but contained no arguments regarding the withdrawal of the appeal (emphasis added). We remarked, moreover, that in a later filing with the Board requesting a stay of deportation received on September 14, 2006, the respondent’s present counsel stated that the previous attorney had failed to consult with the respondent before filing a waiver of appeal. We observed, however, that at no point had the respondent herself adequately alleged that she received ineffective assistance of counsel in waiving her appeal. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (we have held that statements by counsel are not evidence). Therefore, we concluded the record supported the finding that she had effectively waived her right to appeal. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993).
 
*2 Instead, the Board “construed the respondent’s statements to be a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance … ¿byº former counsel as to the denial of relief under section 212(c) of the Act,” and as we were without jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen on that specific issue. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600 (BIA 1978) (unless qualified or limited for a specific purpose, a remand is effective for the stated purpose or for consideration of any and all matters which the Immigration Judge deems appropriate in the exercise of his discretion or which are brought to his attention in compliance with the appropriate regulations.). As noted above, the Board’s remand was limited for a specific purpose.
 
Moreover, we note that an Immigration Judge is not required to hold an evidentiary hearing to adjudicate the respondent’s motion to reopen based on allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel in the presentation of her request for relief under section 212(c) of the Act. On remand, the Immigration Judge adjudicated the respondent’s motion and concluded the respondent’s former counsel had presented “all the evidence and witnesses” necessary to demonstrate her eligibility for relief under section 212(c) of the Act and that the respondent had been afforded a “fair hearing.” See Dakane v. United States Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir. 2005) (Alien must show that counsel’s actions were deficient to point where hearing was fundamentally unfair and alien unable to reasonably present his or her case).
 
The respondent raises no arguments on appeal that would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal from the denial of her motion to reopen is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Board noted that On June 1, 2006, the respondent had filed with the immigration court a “Notice of Itent Not to Appeal the Immigration Judge’s Decision”, signed by the respondent’s former counsel, and containing a letter from the respondent which, in no uncertain terms, indicated the respondent’s clear intent not to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision; and requesting expeditious removal to Jamaica. At that point, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final, and the Board no longer had any jurisdiction over the respondent’s subsequently-filed Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), even though it was filed within thirty days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39.

2007 WL 1125667 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED ASAB UDDIN
File: A96 418 752 - Detroit
February 23, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Munawar H. Sandhu, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On January 20, 2006, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure, noting that the respondent had waived the right to appeal. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). The respondent filed Form EOIR-26 on February 14, 2006, asserting that his former attorney did not properly guide him and asked him to accept voluntary departure despite his marriage to a United States citizen.
 
The record reveals that the respondent initially made some equivocal comments when he was questioned about the withdrawal of the application for adjustment of status (Tr. at 53) and that the Immigration Judge allowed the respondent to consult off the record with his attorney, stating that the respondent’s withdrawal of the application and acceptance of voluntary departure had to be knowing and voluntary (Tr. at 53-54). Thereafter, the respondent personally responded to questioning stating that he was making the decision to withdraw the application for adjustment of status of his own free will after consultation with his attorney and that he had also made the decision to accept a final order of voluntary departure after consultation with his attorney and that no one had forced him to make that decision (Tr. at 55).
 
The respondent now essentially claims that former counsel pressured him into withdrawing his application for adjustment of status and waiving his right to appeal. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). However, despite making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making such a claim. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003). These cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. In this case, the respondent has submitted no affidavit and the statements in the Form EOIR-26 do not show that the attorney has been allowed to respond. There is also no allegation or showing that a bar complaint has been filed. Consequently, we conclude that he has not shown that the waiver of the appeal was due to ineffective assistance of his former counsel.
 
*2 After review of the transcript, we find that the respondent made a knowing, intelligent, and voluntary waiver of his right to appeal. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, supra. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1125667 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 1125667 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED ASAB UDDIN
File: AXX XX8 752 - Detroit
February 23, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Munawar H. Sandhu, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On January 20, 2006, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure, noting that the respondent had waived the right to appeal. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). The respondent filed Form EOIR-26 on February 14, 2006, asserting that his former attorney did not properly guide him and asked him to accept voluntary departure despite his marriage to a United States citizen.
 
The record reveals that the respondent initially made some equivocal comments when he was questioned about the withdrawal of the application for adjustment of status (Tr. at 53) and that the Immigration Judge allowed the respondent to consult off the record with his attorney, stating that the respondent’s withdrawal of the application and acceptance of voluntary departure had to be knowing and voluntary (Tr. at 53-54). The respondent now essentially claims that former counsel pressured him into withdrawing his application for adjustment of status and waiving his right to appeal. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). *2 After review of the transcript, we find that the respondent made a knowing, intelligent, and voluntary waiver of his right to appeal. KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Distinguished by IN RE: ISAM JARADAT, BIA, February 16, 2016
24 I. & N. Dec. 87 (BIA), Interim Decision 3552, 2007 WL 549761
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE BOZENA ZMIJEWSKA, RESPONDENT
FILE A70 576 279 - NEW YORK
Decided February 21, 2007
**1 *87 (1) The Board of Immigration Appeals lacks authority to apply an “exceptional circumstances” or other general equitable exception to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.A. § 1229c(d)(1) (West Supp. 2006).
 
(2) An alien has not voluntarily failed to depart the United States under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act when the alien, through no fault of his or her own, was unaware of the voluntary departure order or was physically unable to depart within the time granted.
 
FOR RESPONDENT:
Ruchi Thaker, Esquire
New York, New York
FOR THE DEPARTMENT OF HOMELAND SECURITY:
Michael Horowitz,
Assistant Chief Counsel
BEFORE: Board Panel: HURWITZ, Acting Vice Chairman; HOLMES and GRANT, Board Members.
GRANT, Board Member:
The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has remanded this case with a request that we clarify whether, under section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c (2000), “Congress intended to permit courts, in the exercise of their equitable discretion, to grant exceptions to the 10-year ineligibility period imposed as a penalty for failure to comply with voluntary departure orders.” Zmijewska v. Gonzales, 426 F.3d 99, 101 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
The court further requested that we reconsider the respondent’s claims “in light of the Government’s revelation at oral argument that [her] voluntary departure deadline was in fact November 8, 2002, rather than November 9, 2002, and therefore that [her] Board-accredited representative had not informed her of the existence of the BIA’s voluntary departure order by the time her departure deadline passed.” Id. at 100-01.
 
As discussed below, Congress has provided certain exceptions to the penalties for overstaying a period of voluntary departure. The statute now limits application of the penalty provisions to situations in which a respondent *88 “voluntarily fails to depart the United States within the time period specified.” Section 240B(d)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C.A. § 1229c(d)(1) (West Supp. 2006) (emphasis added). Thus, assuming the truth of the respondent’s allegations regarding her accredited representative’s delay in informing her of our voluntary departure order, we find that the respondent did not voluntarily fail to depart within the period for voluntary departure. We will therefore vacate our prior decision denying the respondent’s motions to reopen and reconsider, grant her motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
  
I. FACTUAL AND PROCEDURAL HISTORY
 
**2 The respondent entered the United States in July 1988 and remained beyond the 6 months authorized by her visitor’s visa. The Immigration and Naturalization Service (“INS”), now the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), initiated removal proceedings in September 1999.
 
Assisted by an accredited representative, the respondent conceded in Immigration Court that she was subject to removal for staying beyond the period authorized by her nonimmigrant visa. In a decision dated June 13, 2000, the Immigration Judge pretermitted her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1) (2000), for lack of a qualifying relative. In addition, because the respondent’s pending request for a labor certification had not yet been approved, the Immigration Judge found her ineligible for adjustment of status and any other relief from removal other than voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge issued an order granting the respondent 60 days for voluntary departure from the United States, with an alternate order of removal to Poland. The Immigration Judge also instructed the respondent that if she failed to depart within the time afforded, she would be barred from relief from removal “unless there were exceptional circumstances beyond [her] control as to why [she] didn’t comply with that order of voluntary departure such as being very sick or someone dying in [her] family or some other type of exceptional circumstance.”
 
The respondent timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s pretermission of her request for cancellation of removal. On October 9, 2002, we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal and granted her 30 days for voluntary departure.
 
On January 7, 2003, after learning that her appeal had been dismissed and obtaining new counsel, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance by her former representative for failing to seek a remand for adjustment of status while her appeal was pending with the Board and for failing to notify her of the Board’s decision until after the period we granted her for voluntary departure had passed. The respondent attached to her motion to reopen an approved labor certification and an approved employment *89 visa petition. The relevant Visa Bulletins indicate that visas with the respondent’s priority date were available for the respondent’s employment category in March 2002.
 
On April 29, 2003, we denied the motion to reopen. We found that the motion was untimely and, alternatively, that the respondent, as a consequence of overstaying our grant of voluntary departure, was ineligible for adjustment of status under the 10-year bar in section 240B of the Act. We also rejected the respondent’s claim that “exceptional circumstances” excused her from the 10-year bar, finding that although such an exception was included in former section 242B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b (1994), a respondent in removal proceedings instituted on or after April 1, 1997, is subject to the terms of section 240B which, unlike former section 242B, does not contain an “exceptional circumstances” exception for failing to comply with a voluntary departure order.
 
**3 The respondent then filed a timely motion for reconsideration, pointing out that we had erred in finding that the motion to reopen was untimely and requesting that we recognize an exception to the voluntary departure penalty when failure to depart within the time provided is the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. On September 26, 2003, we issued a decision acknowledging that the motion to reopen had been timely filed but denying reconsideration on the basis that “[t]he prior decision found that under section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c, the respondent had not shown ‘exceptional circumstances’ for her failure to comply with the voluntary departure order.”
 
The respondent appealed both the denial of the motion to reopen and the denial of the motion to reconsider to the Second Circuit. In its remand to the Board, the court framed the issue as follows:
The question presented is whether, in light of 8 U.S.C. § 1229c, [the respondent’s] failure to comply with a voluntary departure order renders her statutorily ineligible to receive an adjustment of status arising out of an employment offer, see 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), or whether [she] may instead seek equitable relief from the provisions of 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d) in the extraordinary circumstances presented here-namely, where: (1) [she] relied on erroneous representations of an immigration agent advising her not to seek adjustment of status after receiving a labor certification but prior to the BIA’s order of voluntary departure; (2) [her] Board-accredited representative failed to notify her of the existence of the BIA’s voluntary removal order until the day after she was required to depart the United States; and (3) an Immigration Judge (“IJ”) informed (or misinformed) [the respondent] on the record that even if she failed to depart voluntarily in accordance with a BIA order, she still could avoid becoming statutorily ineligible for further relief by demonstrating that “there were exceptional circumstances beyond [her] control.”
 
Zmijewska v. Gonzales, supra, at 101 (footnote omitted).
  
*90 II. ANALYSIS
  
A. Statutory Language
 
We begin with the statutory language of the penalty provisions for failing to depart within the time afforded for voluntary departure. Penalties for failing to depart pursuant to a grant of voluntary departure were first imposed in 1990 with the enactment of section 545(a) of the Immigration Act of 1990, Pub. L. No. 101-649, 104 Stat. 4978, 5061, 5064 (effective Nov. 29, 1990). The statute, which was codified at former section 242B(e)(2)(A) of the Act, provided, in relevant part:
**4 [A]ny alien … who remains in the United States after the scheduled date of departure, other than because of exceptional circumstances, shall not be eligible for relief described in paragraph (5) [including adjustment of status] for a period of 5 years after the scheduled date of departure or the date of unlawful reentry, respectively.
 
(Emphasis added.) The initial penalty provision thus contained an “exceptional circumstances” exception for failing to depart within the allotted time. The term “exceptional circumstances” was defined to include “exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Former section 242B(f)(2)of the Act. The statute also required written notice in English and Spanish and oral notice in a language the alien understands of the consequences of failing to depart within the time afforded, other than because of exceptional circumstances. Former section 242B(e)(2)(B) of the Act.
 
In 1996, Congress amended the penalty provision for failure to depart pursuant to a grant of voluntary departure in a number of ways. See Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Div. C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, § 304(a)(3), 110 Stat. 3009-546, 3009-587, 3009-596, (“IIRIRA”). Most significantly, the IIRIRA eliminated the “exceptional circumstances” exception. It also added a new clause specifying that the penalties apply to an alien who “fails voluntarily to depart.”1 Id. § 304(a)(3), 110 Stat. at 3009-597. The newly enacted section of the statute regarding penalties for failure to depart provided as follows:
If an alien is permitted to depart voluntarily under this section and fails voluntarily to depart the United States within the time period specified, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under this section and sections 240A,  *91 245, 248, and 249. The order permitting the alien to depart voluntarily shall inform the alien of the penalties under this subsection.
 
Section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d) (2000) (emphasis added). We have never directly addressed in a precedent decision the meaning of the phrase “fails voluntarily to depart the United States” contained in section 240B(d).
 
In January 2006, after the Second Circuit remanded this case, Congress amended section 240B(d) of the Act to make two changes relevant to the voluntary departure penalty provision. See Violence Against Women and Department of Justice Reauthorization Act of 2005, Pub. L. No. 109-162, § 812, 119 Stat. 2960, 3057 (enacted Jan. 5, 2006) (“VAWA”). Section 240B(d) of the Act now provides as follows:
  
Civil Penalty for Failure to Depart
  
(1) In General
 
**5 Subject to paragraph (2), if an alien is permitted to depart voluntarily under this section and voluntarily fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, the alien —
(A) shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000; and
 
(B) shall be ineligible, for a period of 10 years, to receive any further relief under this section and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249.
 
 
  
(2) Application of VAWA Protections
 
The restrictions on relief under paragraph (1) shall not apply to relief under section 240A or 245 on the basis of a petition filed by a VAWA self-petitioner, or a petition filed under section 240A(b)(2), or under section 244(a)(3) (as in effect prior to March 31, 1977), if the extreme cruelty or battery was at least one central reason for the alien’s overstaying the grant of voluntary departure.
 
  
(3) Notice of Penalties
 
The order permitting an alien to depart voluntarily shall inform the alien of the penalties under this subsection.
 
 
In enacting the 2006 amendments, Congress recognized that some VAWA applicants may have overstayed a period of voluntary departure for reasons that justify an exception to the penalty provisions. Notably, the VAWA exception is limited to applicants who demonstrate that extreme cruelty or battery was a central reason for overstaying the grant of voluntary departure. Congress also changed the wording of the introductory clause of section 240B(d) from “fails voluntarily to depart” to “voluntarily fails to depart.” This change in wording clarifies that the phrase was not intended to apply to every alien who failed to depart within the voluntary departure period but was meant to limit the penalties to only those aliens whose failure to depart was voluntary.
  
*92 B. Equitable Exceptions to the Voluntary Departure Penalty Provisions
 
Before reaching the question whether the respondent in this case “voluntarily” failed to depart, we must first address the issue presented on remand, i.e., whether courts may exercise equitable discretion to grant exceptions to the penalty provisions of section 240B(d). We understand that the court’s request requires us to consider whether the statute may be interpreted to afford agency and court authority to recognize equitable exceptions, in addition to the exceptions spelled out in the statute. For the reasons specified below, we find that Congress has not given the Board or the courts authority to recognize equitable exceptions to the section 240B(d) bar beyond those specifically provided in the voluntary departure provisions.
 
The question of equitable exceptions to the voluntary departure penalties involves considerations somewhat different from those relevant to the question of equitable exceptions to statutes of limitations. See Iavorski v. U.S. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129 (2d Cir. 2000). Unlike a statute of limitations, the voluntary departure grant involves a quid pro quo arrangement between an alien and the Government of the United States. Banda-Ortiz v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 387, 389 (5th Cir. 2006) (“Voluntary departure is the result of an agreed-upon exchange of benefits between an alien and the Government.”)In return for departing within the time afforded for voluntary departure, an alien avoids certain adverse consequences of a removal order. See, e.g., section 212(a)(9)(A)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(A)(ii) (2000)(rendering inadmissible for 10 years an alien who has been ordered removed from the United States.) The voluntary departure agreement comes with warnings of the consequences of failing to depart within the time granted.2 Therefore, exceptions to the penalty provisions, unlike exceptions to a statute of limitation, are included in the statutory framework setting forth the terms of the agreement.
 
**6 In the original version of the statute, Congress afforded a rather broad and open-ended equitable exception to the penalty provisions for aliens who could demonstrate “exceptional circumstances,” which was defined to include “serious illness” and other “compelling circumstances.” See, e.g., Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (finding that ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to “exceptional circumstances” in the context of a motion to reopen an in absentia removal order).
 
*93 The congressional repeal of the “exceptional circumstances” exception to the voluntary departure penalty soon after our decision in Matter of Grijalva, supra, and its replacement with a “voluntariness” test strongly suggest that Congress did not intend to allow the Board and the courts to create and apply a set of equitable exceptions that would amount to a substitute version of the repealed “exceptional circumstances” exception. Notably, at the same time that Congress repealed the “exceptional circumstances” exception for voluntary departure penalties, it left intact the same exception for rescission of in absentia orders of removal for failure to appear. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (2000). Additionally, the wording of the recently enacted VAWA exception, which requires extreme cruelty or battery to be “a central reason” for a VAWA applicant’s failure to depart, indicates that Congress intended to limit the exceptions to the penalties for failure to depart to those situations specifically described in the statute, i.e., the “voluntariness” provision and the VAWA exception.
 
Given the contractual nature of the voluntary departure agreement, the specificity of the exceptions to the penalty provision now provided by Congress, and the amendments to the statute, most particularly the repeal of the more general “exceptional circumstances” exception, we find that Congress intended to foreclose the Board and the courts from applying an open-ended equitable exception to the penalties for failing to depart within the time for voluntary departure.3
  
C. Application of the “Voluntariness” Exception
 
We now turn to the question whether the respondent “voluntarily” failed to depart within the period afforded for voluntary departure. The term “voluntarily” ordinarily refers to conduct that is “brought about of one’s own accord or by free choice.” The Random House Dictionary of the English Language 1600 (unabridged ed. 1973). Congress has used the phrase “voluntarily fails” in a number of provisions. See, e.g., 10 U.S.C.A. § 2192a(e) (West Supp. 2006) (a participant who “voluntarily fails” to complete a Department of Defense education program shall provide a refund to the United States in an appropriate amount); 20 U.S.C.A. § 1927(a)(3)(West 2005) (an individual who “voluntarily fails” to complete a term of service with the Capitol police must repay recruitment and relocation bonus *94 on a pro rata basis); 22 U.S.C. § 3657(c)(2) (2000) (an employee who “voluntarily fails” to complete the required period of employment must repay recruitment and retention bonuses). In each instance, the phrase “voluntarily fails” refers to a failure to complete an act or requirement within the actor’s control. See Favreau v. United States, 317 F.3d 1346 (D.C. Cir. 2002) (approving recoupment of enlistment bonuses when service members were unable to meet obesity and physical fitness requirements by examining the voluntariness of the underlying conduct of the employee, rather than the voluntariness of termination). Under the terms of section 240B(d) of the Act, a respondent who, through no fault of her own, remains unaware of the grant of voluntary departure until after the period for voluntary departure has expired cannot be said to have “voluntarily” failed to depart within the period of voluntary departure.
 
**7 We emphasize that the “voluntariness” exception is not a substitute for the repealed “exceptional circumstances” exception. It is a much narrower exception limited to situations in which an alien, through no fault of his or her own, is unaware of the voluntary departure order or is physically unable to depart. It would not include situations in which departure within the period granted would involve exceptional hardships to the alien or close family members. Nor would lack of funds for departure be considered an involuntary failure to depart.
 
The respondent claims that she did not depart the United States within the 30-day period for voluntary departure because her accredited representative failed to inform her of our October 9, 2002, order until after the expiration of her voluntary departure period. When she learned of the order on day 31, she also learned that she had become eligible for adjustment of status during the course of her appeal and could have sought a remand for adjustment of status several months before we issued our decision dismissing her appeal and granting voluntary departure.
 
Ordinarily, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by (1) an affidavit setting out the agreement entered into with counsel, (2) proof that notice of the allegations and an opportunity to respond has been provided to counsel whose representation was allegedly ineffective, and (3) the filing of a complaint with appropriate disciplinary authorities or an explanation for the failure to file a complaint. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In this case, the respondent was represented by an accredited representative, not an attorney. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1292.1(a)(4), 1292.2(d) (2006). During the course of her appeal, the respondent submitted a copy of a formal complaint provided to her accredited representative and apparently offered him an opportunity to respond. Although we have not yet decided the question whether the Lozada requirements should be strictly applied to an accredited representative, we find *95 that the steps taken by the respondent in this case satisfy the concerns underlying the Lozada requirements.4
 
Assuming the truth of the respondent’s allegations regarding her accredited representative’s conduct, we find that she has shown that she did not “voluntarily” fail to depart within the period of voluntary departure granted by the Board.5 In addition, the respondent in this case was erroneously instructed by the Immigration Judge that the penalty provisions would not apply in the event that she could demonstrate exceptional circumstances for having failed to depart within the time afforded.6 At the time the respondent learned that she had overstayed the 30-day period of voluntary departure granted by the Board, she could reasonably have believed that the ineffective assistance of her accredited representative, and her apparent eligibility for adjustment of status during the course of the appeal, could satisfy an exceptional circumstances exception. See, e.g., Matter of Grijalva, supra (holding that ineffective assistance of counsel that causes an alien to fail to appear for a hearing may amount to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of former section 242B(f)(2) of the Act).
 
**8 Finally, we find that the respondent acted with due diligence in locating new counsel and filing a timely motion to reopen proceedings within 2 months of learning of our order dismissing her appeal and of her eligibility for adjustment of status in removal proceedings. We will therefore vacate our prior decisions denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and motion to reconsider, grant the motion to reopen, and remand the proceedings to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The October 9, 2002, and April 29, 2003, decisions of the Board are vacated.
 
*96 FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

1

The IIRIRA also increased the period of ineligibility for the specified forms of relief from removal from 5 years to 10 years, added a bond requirement, and eliminated the requirement of oral notice of the consequences of failing to depart within the period for voluntary departure.

2

The statute provides that the order permitting an alien to depart voluntarily “shall inform the alien of the penalties under this subsection.” Section 240B(d)(3) of the Act. There is no dispute in this case that the order of voluntary departure provided notice of the consequences of failing to voluntarily depart. Nor is there any question in this case concerning the posting of a voluntary departure bond. See Matter of Diaz-Ruacho, 24 I&N Dec. 47 (BIA 2006) (holding that an alien who fails to post the voluntary departure bond is not subject to penalties for failure to depart within the time specified for voluntary departure).

3

Some courts have ruled that a timely motion to reopen filed before the expiration of a period of voluntary departure tolls the running of the voluntary departure period until the agency rules on the motion. See, e.g., Azarte v. Ashcroft, 394 F.3d 1278 (9th Cir. 2005) (reaching this result after considering the motion to reopen provisions of section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7) (2000), in conjunction with the voluntary departure provisions of section 240B(d)); cf. Dekoladenu v. Gonzales, 459 F.3d 500 (4th Cir. 2006) (finding that the plain language of section 240B(d) precludes tolling of the voluntary departure period upon the filing of a motion to reopen).

4

Had the respondent been represented by an attorney, we would have expected compliance with the third prong of Matter of Lozada, supra, i.e., the filing of a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities or an explanation why such a complaint was not filed.

5

Ultimately, we leave the issue of the truth of the respondent’s allegations regarding her accredited representative’s conduct to the Immigration Judge on remand.

6

At the hearing, the Immigration Judge provided the respondent with the following explanation of her right to appeal to the Board and the consequences of failing to comply with the voluntary departure order:
If an individual has a period of voluntary departure and does not leave by that date or the date given by the Appeals Board, then an alternate order of removal, in this case to Poland would go into effect. If you didn’t leave under those circumstances, then you would be barred from reopening your case for 10 years to apply for any form of relief and if you are ultimately removed or deported from the United States, you’d also be barred for 10 years without obtaining a special waiver. All of that would happen unless there were exceptional circumstances beyond your control as to why you didn’t comply with that order of voluntary departure such as being very sick or someone dying in your family or some other type of exceptional circumstance.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL ENCARNACION SOSA-PADILLA A.K.A. MIGUEL ENCARNACION SOSA
File: A91 834 3711 - Harlingen
February 21, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Okon J. Usoro, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Evans,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on July 18, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent has primarily reiterated the arguments that were made in his last motion. We considered these arguments before rendering our previous decision in this case and we decline to revisit them. A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
In the motion, the respondent maintains that his motion to reopen was filed with the Harlingen Immigration Court on September 7, 2005, (not as the Board referenced on March 16, 2006) but it was rescinded by the Immigration Court. While that may be true, it does not explain why the respondent waited until then to allege ineffective assistance of former counsel, when he knew of the in absentia order as early as 1998, at which time he filed a previous motion to reopen through new counsel. Consequently, we find no error in our prior decision denying the motion to reopen. See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir.2000)(the decision to reopen proceedings is a discretionary decision, and this court applies a highly deferential abuse-of-discretion standard when reviewing the BIA’s denial of a motion to reopen).
 
The respondent also now alleges that his two previous attorneys who filed motions to reopen should have addressed the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel by the respondent’s first counsel, Lionel Perez. Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider, page3. To the extent the respondent now alleges ineffective assistance by the two previous attorneys, we note that the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has moved to consolidate A28 342 452 (the alien number assigned to the respondent in deportation proceedings) with A91 834 371(the alien number assigned to the respondent in removal proceedings). The DHS motion to consolidate is granted.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AL ROUMY, SAID
File: AXX-XX6-342 - Detroit
February 20, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mann, George P.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated August 28, 2006. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order on the merits in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 28, 2006. In that decision, we considered the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to grant him a continuance to obtain additional evidence which included statements from family members, but found that neither the respondent nor his counsel specifically requested a continuance and that the respondent was aware of the need to produce corroborating evidence. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must generally be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before November 27, 2606. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until December 14, 2006. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
The evidence submitted in support of reopening includes articles concerning country conditions in Lebanon and evidence from the respondent and his family. The respondent has not contended that the nature of any persecution he reportedly fears has changed in character, but only that it has varied in scope and degree. The evidence submitted does not establish that the respondent is entitled to an exception to the filing requirements for reopening based on an asylum claim arising from materially changed circumstances in Lebanon.
 
The respondent, who was represented throughout the appellate process and during the issuance of multiple decisions by this Board by an attorney, now attempts to raise for the first time a claim of ineffective assistance by an accredited representative at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. There is no allegation or showing that the respondent has given the accredited representative notice of this claim or otherwise complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, as the respondent has not shown due diligence in pursuing this claim of ineffective assistance, we cannot conclude that there is a basis for equitable tolling of the filing date for the motion to reopen. See also Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 413 (6th Cir.2003) (An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence; prejudice must be shown on ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1129168 (BIA)

2007 WL 1153988 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR LUIS FRIAS-ORTEGA
File: A44 924 627 - Miami
February 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosenny Burgos, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On September 19, 2005, an Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned his request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(a), because he failed to timely file the application for relief, as ordered by the court. The Immigration Judge declined to reopen or reconsider his decision on October 19, 2005. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on June 28, 2006. The respondent, who is from the Dominican Republic, filed an untimely motion to reopen on December 19, 2006. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent blames the “Hispanic Coalition” for his cancellation application being filed late. However, the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that the motion to reopen period is “mandatory and jurisdictional”, and not subject to equitable tolling, even where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is raised. Abdi v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1149-50 (11th Cir. 2005); see also Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999)(concerning motion to reopen in absentia order).
 
We note that, in any event, the respondent fails to show diligence in bringing his claims against the Hispanic Coalition. The Immigration Judge set a September 16, 2005, deadline for the respondent to file his cancellation of removal application (Tr. at 7). The respondent states in his affidavit that the Hispanic Coalition agreed to complete the necessary forms, then provide them to an attorney to review (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 8). The respondent states that the Hispanic Coalition gave him the forms on September 19, 2005, and he filed the documents with the Immigration Court on September 21, 2005 (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 8-10). The Hispanic Coalition thereafter prepared the motion to reopen submitted to the Immigration Judge, the respondent states, and then helped him file the appeal with the Board (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 11-13). The appeal was denied, as stated, on June 28, 2006. Yet the pending motion was not filed for another 6 months. The respondent claims in his affidavit that a representative from the Hispanic Coalition was “rude” when the respondent sought to follow up on his case after we dismissed the appeal on June 28, 2006 (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 14). Yet the respondent fails to explain why he waited so long to file the pending motion with the Board, particularly where his affidavit is dated July 26, 2006.
 
*2 Given the respondent’s lack of diligence in bringing his complaints against the Hispanic Coalition, we will not reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a).
 
The pending motion is, therefore denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1153988 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: HECTOR LUIS FRIAS-ORTEGA
File: AXX XX4 627 - Miami
February 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosenny Burgos, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On September 19, 2005, an Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned his request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(a), because he failed to timely file the application for relief, as ordered by the court. The Immigration Judge declined to reopen or reconsider his decision on October 19, 2005. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on June 28, 2006. The respondent, who is from the Dominican Republic, filed an untimely motion to reopen on December 19, 2006. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent blames the “Hispanic Coalition” for his cancellation application being filed late. However, the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that the motion to reopen period is “mandatory and jurisdictional”, and not subject to equitable tolling, even where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is raised. Abdi v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1149-50 (11th Cir. 2005); see also Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999)(concerning motion to reopen in absentia order).
 
We note that, in any event, the respondent fails to show diligence in bringing his claims against the Hispanic Coalition. The Immigration Judge set a September 16, 2005, deadline for the respondent to file his cancellation of removal application (Tr. at 7). The respondent states in his affidavit that the Hispanic Coalition agreed to complete the necessary forms, then provide them to an attorney to review (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 8). The respondent states that the Hispanic Coalition gave him the forms on September 19, 2005, and he filed the documents with the Immigration Court on September 21, 2005 (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 8-10). The Hispanic Coalition thereafter prepared the motion to reopen submitted to the Immigration Judge, the respondent states, and then helped him file the appeal with the Board (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 11-13). The appeal was denied, as stated, on June 28, 2006. Yet the pending motion was not filed for another 6 months. The respondent claims in his affidavit that a representative from the Hispanic Coalition was “rude” when the respondent sought to follow up on his case after we dismissed the appeal on June 28, 2006 (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 14). Yet the respondent fails to explain why he waited so long to file the pending motion with the Board, particularly where his affidavit is dated July 26, 2006.
 
*2 Given the respondent’s lack of diligence in bringing his complaints against the Hispanic Coalition, we will not reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a).
 
The pending motion is, therefore denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1153988 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 11th Cir., January 28, 2008
2007 WL 1153991 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALOME MELSEIO MONTANO CISNEROS
File: A95 129 071 - Atlanta
February 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Philip A. Turtletaub, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rhoshunda R. Rhodes,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 6, 2006. The record reflects that the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, which set forth the consequences for a failure to appear. On August 19, 2004, the respondent requested a new hearing date because he was unable to get into the court building to attend his hearing. The Immigration Judge granted his request. The respondent failed to appear for his next hearing on September 17, 2004. He was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge granted reopening. However, the respondent failed to appear for his removal hearing on February 8, 2006. He was again ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia.
 
On July 13, 2006, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. He claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion was not supported by an affidavit from the respondent, or any evidence. The Immigration Judge found that statements of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). She further found that there was no indication that the respondent had been represented by the person he claimed had ineffectively assisted him, and the motion was not supported by an application for the relief sought upon reopening. The Immigration Judge denied reopening.
 
The respondent submitted another motion to reopen on August 3, 2006. He offered evidence that a complaint had been filed against the person who claimed to be an attorney and the notary who assisted him. He also offered an affidavit, an application for cancellation of removal, and evidence to support that application. The respondent claimed that he had appeared for a hearing on August 18, 2004. He stated that he failed to appear for the hearing on February 8, 2006, because the person who claimed to be an attorney said that his case would be transferred and that he did not need to appear. He said that the current whereabouts of that person are unknown so he can not contact him, but that he has otherwise complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had been given notice of the hearing date and previously warned of the consequences for a failure to appear. She found that the respondent’s decision to ignore that warning and follow the advice of the person who assisted him did not amount to exceptional circumstances. The Immigration Judge appropriately denied the respondent’s motion. Moreover, the respondent’s motion to reopen is number barred. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). We note that the respondent’s proceedings were previously reopened to give him additional opportunities to appear, and we find no merit to his claim that he was denied due process. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 1153991 (BIA)

2007 WL 1154067 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DREPAUL GULCHARRAN A.K.A. MAHESH
File: A77 050 611 - New York
February 7, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven A. Mundie, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated November 30, 2006, in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in absentia. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” is defined by the Act as “exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
A review of the record reflects that the Immigration Judge’s order of removal was entered in absentia on September 15, 1999. The respondent failed to file his motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the order of removal, as is required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. Therefore, the motion was correctly found untimely. In his motion, the respondent stated that he failed to appear because he was fearful of being removed. We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s fear of being removed does not constitute “exceptional circumstances.”
 
In his motion before the Immigration Judge, the respondent intimates that his wife’s former counsel failed to file a timely motion to reopen. To the extent this argument is based on ineffective assistance of counsel, it is not supported as required under Matter of Lozada, 19 I& N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Thus, the respondent’s argument fails.
 
The respondent also sought to reopen proceedings to apply for adjustment of status. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).1 Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision, and after September 30, 1996. He is accordingly precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.
 
*2 Nor do we find any error in the Immigration Judge’s refusal to reopen sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (The Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Becoming eligible for legal status in this country or relief from deportation after a final administrative order has been entered is common and does not, in itself, constitute an exceptional circumstance warranting consideration of an untimely motion. To hold otherwise would vitiate the statutory and regulatory deadlines, which are designed to bring finality to immigration proceedings. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (motions to reopen are especially disfavored in immigration proceedings because every delay works to the advantage of the deportable alien). As a general rule, sua sponte authority is used sparingly and it is not used as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373, 380 n.9 (A.G. 2002). Although the respondent maintains that his lawful permanent resident wife would suffer hardship if he is removed, we do not find that the respondent has established that an exceptional circumstance exists to justify reopening these proceedings sua sponte. For the foregoing reasons, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that Matter of M-S-, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings and involved the interpretation of sections 242B(e)(1) and (5) of the Act, relating to the limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a deportation hearing. However, as section 240(b)(7) of the Act sets forth a similar limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a removal hearing, we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of M-S-, supra, by analogy.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: DREPAUL GULCHARRAN A.K.A. MAHESH

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL

Steven A. Mundie, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
