2007 WL 926773 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EMETERIA J. RAMIREZ-MEDRANO
File: AXX XX3 537 - Imperial
February 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James T. Stroud, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(d)(11) waiver
 
This matter was first before the Board on March 1, 2005, following the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying her application for cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). At that time, the Board remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s eligibility for a waiver under section 212(d)(11) of the Act. See 8 U.S.C. § 1182(d)(11) (providing for discretionary waiver of inadmissibility, based upon alien smuggling of family member, for humanitarian purposes, to assure family unity, or when it is otherwise in the public interest). On September 14, 2005, after holding additional hearings, the Immigration Judge denied the waiver, and on October 25, 2006, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision.1 The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings alleging ineffective assistance of counsel at her hearing on her application for the section 212(d)(11) waiver. The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen must, among other things, state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). An alien who seeks to remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a heavy burden of proving that if proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
At the outset, we find that the respondent has complied with the technical requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has also presented evidence that her former counsel, Juan Molina, failed to call available witnesses who were willing to testify about the respondent’s good moral character; attempts to further her education; church work; volunteer work; devotion to friends, family, and the community; and to the hardship her deportation would cause to her family. The respondent has submitted evidence that Attorney Molina has been disciplined and suspended by the State Bar of California on several occasions. We further observe that Attorney Molina was suspended from practice before this Board from April 11, 2006, until August 1, 2006.
 
*2 The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case originates, has stated that an inquiry into whether counsel provided ineffective assistance must begin with determining whether competent counsel would have acted otherwise before examining whether an alien was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective actions of counsel. See Maravilla-Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). In addition, “substantial prejudice” must be shown; such prejudice means that the alleged ineffectiveness affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
Based upon the record before us, we must conclude that competent counsel would have called available witnesses to testify on behalf of the respondent given that the issue presented to the Immigration Judge was whether the respondent should be granted the relief she sought as a matter of discretion. Notwithstanding this fact, however, we find that the respondent has not established substantial prejudice resulting from the failure to call the witnesses in this case. This is because we find that the outcome of the case would not have been different had the witnesses been called and had they provided the testimony described in their affidavits.
 
The respondent was seeking a section 212(d)(11) waiver of inadmissibility after it was determined she was subject to removal for assisting her adult son to enter the United States illegally. The record establishes that the person the respondent attempted to smuggle into the United States was someone the respondent knew was a convicted criminal who had been deported on multiple occasions and who had been involved in the sale of illegal drugs (Tr. #1 at 66-68, 143-44, 158-60; Tr. #2 at 8, 12). In her original proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent denied any involvement in or even knowledge of the smuggling (Tr. # 1 at 131-33, 140-41). However, after the Board’s remand for consideration of her eligibility for a waiver, she finally changed her story and admitted to knowing about the smuggling of her son (Tr. #2 at 3-7). This son had been living in the house that the respondent owned in Mexico, had been renting out a portion of the house, and had various family members living in the houses around him (Tr. #1 at 59, 65, 68-69, 127, 212).
 
At the time of her hearings, the respondent still owned her house in Mexico (Tr. #1 at 127, 212; Tr. #2 at 4, 20-21). She stated that she had two children, four siblings, and her parents living in Mexico (Tr. #1 at 223; Tr. #2 at 8). Prior to being placed in proceedings, the respondent traveled monthly and sometimes more often to Mexico to visit family (Tr. #1 at 21, 62; Tr. #2 at 24). She testified that she could relocate to Mexico with little difficulty (Tr. #2 at 24-25). The respondent admitted at both of her hearings that she had failed to pay income tax in the United States (Tr. #1 at 193-96, 214; Tr. #2 at 25). She testified that she worked for pay by watching after children and dogs and by cleaning houses, and that she managed an apartment complex, for which she received housing (Tr. #1 at 195-96, 212-14).
 
*3 Even assuming the respondent had presented witnesses at her second hearing who would have testified about her good works, devotion to friends, family, and the community, attempts to educate herself, and to the hardship her deportation would cause to her family, we conclude that the outcome of this case would not have been different. In part, this is because the record already contains letters from friends and clients of the respondent stating that she and her husband are hardworking, that she provides good child care, and that she is very nice and honest (Tr. #1 at 195-97; Exh. 5). The record also contains a letter from the respondent’s church about her volunteer work (Tr. #1 at 198; Exh. 6). Thus, the evidence the respondent seeks to have considered appears to be cumulative.
 
Furthermore, the Immigration Judge exhibited specific awareness of the respondent’s equities in his decision of August 29, 2003 (addressing the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal) (I.J. #1 at 13-16). In that decision, the Immigration Judge extensively discussed various positive and negative factors in the case, including the respondent’s family ties in the United States and Mexico; the respondent’s long residence in the United States; the respondent’s numerous visits to Mexico to see family; the emotional and financial hardship her removal would cause to her husband and other family and to herself; the lost opportunities the respondent would experience; the respondent’s prospects for finding employment in Mexico based upon her experience and skills; the respondent’s lack of a criminal record; the respondent’s volunteer work with her church; the respondent’s ability to speak English; the respondent’s sense of duty to the son she smuggled and the special relationship found to exist between parents and children; the respondent’s refusal at the border to provide a sworn statement; and the fact that the respondent and her son duped the respondent’s husband into participating in the smuggling.
 
Of additional importance to our decision today are the particular circumstances surrounding the respondent’s smuggling of her son. The individual smuggled was a man whom the respondent knew had been involved in illegal drag activity and had been deported from the United States multiple times. Thus, the act of smuggling was directly contrary to the specific efforts of the United States to keep this particular individual out of the country. Moreover, there was no testimony that the smuggling was done out of any extraordinary need. The son was an adult with a place to live in Mexico and with other family members living nearby. We note that the waiver for which the respondent applied is granted for humanitarian purposes, to assure family unity, or when it is otherwise in the public interest. It is clearly not in the public interest to grant the waiver to one who intentionally smuggles in someone known to be involved in illegal drugs and who was previously deported numerous times for that involvement.
 
*4 Also significant is the respondent’s failure to tell the truth about the smuggling until after she had been in proceedings for a number of years. Additionally, as the Immigration Judge found, the respondent’s failure to pay income tax, particularly in light of her family’s use of public services is significant. She testified that she came to the United states to find a better way of life for herself and her three children and she acknowledged that she remained here illegally and enrolled all of her children (also here illegally) in public schools, and then she further acknowledged that she had failed to pay income tax, even after being granted lawful permanent resident status through an amnesty program (Tr. #1 at 193-94, 211-14, 228; Tr. #2 at 16-20). When asked on July 20, 2005, about her taxes for the prior two years, she indicated that she did not want to pay her taxes until her “situation” was in order (Tr. #2 at 25).
 
Given the particular circumstances of the smuggling in this case; the respondent’s failure over an extended period of time to admit her actions; the respondent’s use of public services for her family but failure to pay her share of income tax, even after having been in proceedings for several years; the fact that the record already contains evidence of the positive factors in this case; and the Immigration Judge’s awareness of the respondent’s equities, we cannot conclude that the additional evidence the respondent seeks to be considered would have made a difference in the outcome of this case. The negative factors in this case greatly outweigh the respondent’s equities. Because the respondent has not presented new evidence with her motion that would likely change the result in the case, her motion to reopen will be denied. See Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
  
David B. Holmes
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The transcripts from both hearings appear in the record. The hearings are distinguished in this decision by references to “Tr. #1” and “Tr. #2.”

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUNDAY ELUEMUNOR IJEH
File: AXX XX9 358 - Bloomington
February 5, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irena I. Karpinski, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(9), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(9)º - Nonimmigrant - failed to comply with conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsidering
 
In a decision dated October 22, 1985, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen, but granted the respondent voluntary departure until April 22, 1986, with an alternative order of deportation to Nigeria. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with an Immigration Judge on July 5, 2006, through which he sought reopening in order to again apply for adjustment of his immigration status based on his marriage to a different United States citizen. On July 31, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen both on the respondent’s failure to establish prima facie eligibility for adjustment and as a matter of discretion.
 
On August 30, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider the denial of his motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge issued a decision on September 5, 2006, that found the respondent had appealed the July 2006 decision to the Board and, therefore, the Board had jurisdiction over the motion for reconsideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23 (b)(1). As the contentions on appeal regarding the denial of the motion to reopen are the same as those raised in the motion to reconsider, our discussion of the respondent’s appeal will equally cover the points raised in the motion to reconsider. We therefore will not separately address the motion to reconsider. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that his application for adjustment of status should be reopened on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit has ruled in Nativi-Gomez v. Ashcroft, 344 F.3d 805, 807-09 (8th Cir. 2003), that as an alien has no constitutionally protected liberty interest in discretionary adjustment of status relief, alleged ineffective assistance of counsel in failing to seek discretionary adjustment of status relief in a deportation proceeding did not result in a violation of any recognized due process right and, therefore, could not constitute a basis upon which to reopen a deportation proceeding. See also Guerra-Soto v. Ashcroft, 397 F.3d 637, 640-41 (8th Cir. 2005) (reaffirming that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must fail regarding an entitlement to a form of discretionary relief for the fundamental reason that there is no constitutionally protected interest in seeking discretionary relief). As the respondent’s application for adjustment of status based on his second marriage to a United States citizen is a form of discretionary relief without any constitutionally protected liberty interest, we find that the claim for equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of counsel is without merit. For these reasons, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. The motion to reconsider is denied. We need not address other contentions on appeal in light of our holding above.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
Roger Pauley
  
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 686991 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CECILIA NEREIDA BLOCKER A.K.A. CECILIA BLOCKER-CHRISTMAS A.K.A. CECILIA NEREYDA BLOCKER
File: AXX XX1 368 - Atlanta
February 2, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marsha Williams Mignott, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Morris I. Onyewuchi,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent appeals an Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia deportation order that was entered on April 24, 1991, under former section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b)) (applies when service, or attempted service, of the notice of hearing, is made prior to June 13, 1992). See Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). Although the motion was filed fifteen years after the entry of the in absentia order, there is no time limit to file a motion for purposes of rescinding an in absentia order under former section 242(b) of the Act. Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I&N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999); Matter of Mancera, 22 I&N Dec. 79 (BIA 1998). Therefore, the respondent’s equitable tolling argument based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not necessary to our consideration of the motion to rescind the in absentia order.1
 
Ineffective assistance can constitute “reasonable cause” where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), has been satisfied. See Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). In this case, the record shows that the counsel she has accused, Mr. Suhr, did not represent her until 2001, long after the in absentia order was entered. Her failure to appear at her hearing is therefore unrelated to Mr. Suhr’s representation, and we need not further address her ineffective assistance claim.
 
The reason claimed by the respondent for her failure to appear is that she did not receive the hearing notice. She describes her personal circumstances, which led her to reside with her sister and change her address several times. Contrary to the Immigration Judge’s finding, the respondent did submit an affidavit with the motion (Exh. 12). The affidavit does not specify when she relocated with her sister from the Field Crest Drive address to the address on Gordon Way. However, we point out that, while the front side of the Order to Show Cause states the Field Crest Drive address for the respondent, the certified mail return receipt shows that it was actually addressed and mailed by certified mail to “2920 Apt F Gordon Way” in Fayetteville, where it was successfully delivered on November 28, 1990, as evidenced by her sister’s signature. This discrepancy between the address on the face of the Order to Show Cause and the address on the return receipt was apparently missed by the respondent, the Department of Homeland Security, and the Immigration Judge. Since the Gordon Way address had been successfully used in November 1990 to serve the Order to Show Cause, it is unclear why the old Field Crest Drive address, that was apparently taken from an asylum application that was filed back in 1987, was subsequently used to mail the hearing notice on February 8, 1991. We therefore do not find on this record that the respondent was provided a reasonable opportunity to appear at her April 24, 1991, hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, we will sustain the appeal, rescind the April 24, 1991, in absentia order, reopen the proceedings, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the April 24, 1991, in absentia order is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
  
<Illegible signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

We note that even if a statutory deadline applied to motion seeking to reopen an in absentia order that was entered under former section 242(b), the Eleventh Circuit does not recognize equitable tolling of statutory deadlines. See Ali Abdi v. U.S. Attorney General, 430 F.3d 1148, 50 (11th Cir. 2005) (time periods in statute for motions to reopen immigration proceedings are jurisdictional and mandatory); Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999).

2007 WL 686632 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAIME CASTELLANOS-AVALOS
File: AXX XX6 072 - Tacoma
February 1, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The Board issued a final administrative order in this case on April 12, 2006. On December 26, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen which makes a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Mr. Mahr, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal. The motion is untimely because it was not filed within 90 days of the final order. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Although ineffective assistance can provide a basis for equitably tolling the statutory and regulatory deadline, we are unpersuaded on this record that Mr. Mahr’s representation affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 f.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The respondent is incorrect in his argument that criminal convictions that are not charged in the Notice to Appear cannot be used as a basis for finding him ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has recently held to the contrary. See Salviejo-Fernandez v. Gonzalez, 455 F.3d 1063 (9th Cir. 2006). Further, the respondent has admitted to having two convictions for possession of marijuana in addition to the possession of stolen property charge that was discussed at his removal hearing. These multiple drug possession convictions in themselves bar relief. See section 240A(b)(1)(C) (barring relief if an alien has been convicted of any offense described under section 212(a)(2), section 237(a)(2), or section 237(a)(3)). It is the respondent who bears the burden of proving his eligibility for discretionary relief, and he has not persuasively shown that Mr. Mahr prejudiced the outcome in his case by conceding at the hearing that his convictions preclude him from meeting that burden.1 To the extent that the respondent is seeking, outside of the scope of these proceedings, to remove those convictions, we will not speculate on his prospects for success and they remain a bar to relief under section 240A(b)(1) cancellation of removal because they have not been vacated for a ground that removes the convictions for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006); Matter of Picketing, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). The motion is denied.
  
David B. Holmes
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Indeed, the respondent’s present counsel, in the letter to the Washington State Bar responding to Mr. Mahr’s reply to the disciplinary complaint, points out at page three that the respondent does not have a “realistic chance” to win his case. To the extent that the respondent is now asserting that Mr. Mahr was ineffective in seeking multiple continuances, resulting in the respondent’s detention for a lengthy period in a situation where there was no hope for relief, that is not within our power to correct, as it this possible ineffectiveness neither impacts the respondent’s removability nor his eligibility for relief from removal.

    1  Review Granted, Order Vacated by McLeod v. Mukasey, 9th Cir., July 10, 2008
2007 WL 416805 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MILES IRVEN MCLEOD
File: AXX XX4 640 - Portland, Oregon
January 26, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicole H. Nelson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas L. Day
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
*1 On March 2, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2003, decision which denied the motion to terminate proceedings because his conviction had been vacated. On April 1, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider. We reconsidered the motion and again dismissed the appeal on November 5, 2005. The respondent appealed to the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. On July 24, 2006, the Ninth Circuit remanded the record at the request of the Office of Immigration Litigation. The respondent’s appeal again will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, an approximately 49-year-old native and citizen of Canada, who entered the United States on April 17, 1969, as a lawful permanent resident, was convicted on a plea of guilty, on December 5, 2001, in the Washington County Circuit Court, Oregon, of attempted unlawful manufacture of a controlled substance, marihuana. He was sentenced to 3 years’ probation. A Notice to Appear was served on him on June 24, 2002. On August 15, 2002, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and ineligible for relief. The respondent appealed. He also filed a motion to terminate arguing that his conviction had been vacated and provided an order of the Washington County Circuit Court dated May 22, 2003, vacating his conviction on the basis that he had been given ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board remanded proceedings to the Immigration Court to determine the effect of the Circuit Court order. The Immigration Judge declined to terminate proceedings finding that the State of Oregon had appealed the order vacating the conviction. He found that the Circuit Court order had been stayed by the appeal, and, therefore, the respondent had a conviction for immigration purposes. Subsequently, the Oregon Court of Appeals on June 1, 2004, summarily affirmed the Oregon Circuit Court. On June 20, 2006, the Oregon Supreme Court remanded the appeal to the Oregon Court of Appeals for reconsideration in light of Gonzalez v. Oregon, 340 Or. 452, 134 P.3d 955 (Or. 2006) (Respondent’s Brief on Remand at 6). In that case, the Oregon Supreme Court found an alien who had been informed that he could be deported as a result of his guilty plea in state court had not been subject to ineffective assistance of counsel solely because counsel had not informed him of the high likelihood of his being deported.
 
As recently reaffirmed by the Board in Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006), if the respondent’s conviction were, in fact, vacated by the state court, the conviction would not be effective for immigration purposes. As the Oregon Court of Appeals, to our knowledge, has not yet ruled on remand whether the Circuit Court properly vacated the respondent’s conviction, the case turns on whether the appeal by the State of Oregon acts as a stay on the order of the Circuit Court. The motion to remand proposes that the Board did not address the issue of whether section 138.650 of the Oregon Revised Statutes (ORS) incorporated section 138.160 of the ORS. However, the Board clearly quoted both sections and found that under section 138.650 of the ORS, section 138.160, regarding criminal procedure, applied in this case. “‘The manner of taking the appeal and the scope of review by the Court of Appeals and the Supreme Court shall be the same as that provided by law for appeal in criminal actions, except that ¿certain provisions for transcript and serviceº.’ Section 138.650 of the ORS. Under Oregon Revised Statutes section 138.160, Procedure in Criminal Matters Generally, Appeals; Post-conviction Relief: ‘¿aºn appeal taken by the state stays the effect of the judgment of ¿sicº order in favor of the defendant, so that the release agreement and, if applicable, the security for release, is held for the appearance and surrender of the defendant … but if the defendant is in custody, the defendant may be released by the court … pending the appeal.”’ (BIA 2004 Dec. at 2). The Board did not refer to ORS 138.160 as part of the Post-Conviction Hearings Act in the November 2004 decision (See Respondent’s Brief on Remand at 10).
 
*2 The OIL motion points out that without a remand the Ninth Circuit would be required to interpret Oregon law without the benefit of any input by the Board. The Board does not admit to any particular expertise in regard to Oregon state law. Moreover, the effect of an appeal by the state on a post-conviction motion to vacate appears never to have been a matter of legal controversy within the state. The respondent on remand again has claimed that the order vacating his conviction was not stayed under Oregon law and that a United States District Court had found the respondent does not have a conviction, thus, invoking collateral estoppel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) maintains that the respondent is removable, because the Circuit Court order is stayed and the respondent’s conviction is final for immigration purposes.
 
We conclude, as we did before and for the same reasons, that the Immigration Judge correctly found the respondent removable. The respondent argues that under Schelin v. Maass, 147 Or. App. 351, 936 P.2d 988, rev. den, 325 Or. 446, 940 P.2d 520 (1997), which held that under section 138.650 ORS the “manner of taking the appeal” does not include the taxation of appellate costs and disbursements, the provision for a stay of the lower court order also does not apply. Although the court in that case referenced procedures for filing the appeal under ORS 138.081 as an example of procedures for filing and prosecution of an appeal, the court does not present this as an exhaustive or exclusive example. Id. at 356 n. 3. See also Felkel v. Thompson, 157 Or. App. 218, 970 P.2d 657 (1998), (the court distinguished between the timeliness requirement and the “manner of the appeal” concluding that the manner of the appeal did not include the provision for the timeliness waiver), superceded by, 175 Or. App. 291, 27 P.3d 536 (2001); Holland v. Gladden, 229 Or. 573, 368 P.2d 331 (1962) (service of notice of appeal on the prisoner in a post-conviction proceeding is part of the “manner of taking the appeal”). We conclude that the issue regarding the stay of the lower court’s order is a matter of the manner of the appeal, and that state case law is not in opposition to the interpretation offered by DHS. In addition, the DHS has a copy of a judgment on a probation hearing in this case, entered on October 28, 2004, finding the respondent in violation of his probation, revoking probation, and ordering him committed to jail for 6 months and 1 year PPS. Therefore, it is clear that under Oregon state law the conviction remains in effect during the post- conviction appellate process.
 
As stated in our previous order the United States District Court order directing the release of the respondent does not work a collateral estoppel, among other reasons, because the District Court judge expressly denied that his order had any effect on the deportability of the respondent (Transcript of December 1, 2003, proceedings in the United States District for the District of Oregon, at 8-9). The Judge stated that he was not rendering an opinion on that issue. Id. The Judge also did not decide the effect of the state court appeal of the Circuit Court order. Therefore, this issue was not litigated before the District Court, any admissions of the United States Attorney is not binding, and the doctrine of collateral estoppel does not apply.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 416830 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IRFAN MEHMOOD KHOKHAR
File: AXX XX9 847 - Newark
January 24, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Usman B. Ahmad, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This record was last before us on June 9, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the December 2, 2004, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. We also noted that, although the respondent indicated that he was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he had not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent appealed our decision to the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit. On March 3, 2006, the Department of Justice made an unopposed motion to remand for reconsideration of the decision in light of evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel that was not previously considered.1 In a decision issued on April 25, 2006, the court remanded the record to the Board for consideration of this evidence and for the issuance of a new decision. Accordingly, pursuant to the court’s instructions, we hereby vacate our June 9, 2005, decision and reconsider the respondent’s appeal and motion.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows. The Notice to Appear was filed with the Immigration Court in Newark on March 12, 2003. Numerous continuances were granted to the respondent, apparently to give him the opportunity to pursue his application for a labor certification. On July 1, 2004, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, after the respondent failed to appear for the scheduled hearing. However, on August 12, 2004, the Immigration Judge reopened proceedings, over the objection of the Department of Homeland Security, because he was satisfied that the respondent established an “exceptional circumstance” for his failure to appear.
 
On September 23, 2004, the respondent appeared with former counsel, Tomas Espinosa, and applied only for the relief of post-conclusion voluntary departure in accordance with section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). In response to this request, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure for 60 days, with an alternate order of removal to Pakistan. The respondent did not appeal. On October 25, 2004, the respondent, through former counsel, filed a motion to reopen the proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied in a decision dated December 2, 2004. In that decision, the Immigration Judge emphasized that the respondent “did not offer any proof to the court that he possesses an approved labor certification or an approved visa petition.” The respondent then appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen to the Board on January 3, 2005.
 
*2 The respondent subsequently retained new counsel, and made a motion to reopen on March 18, 2005, based on a claim ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney, Mr. Espinosa. As explained in his brief on appeal, dated April 29, 2005, the respondent asserted that he received ineffective assistance of counsel when Mr. Espinosa applied for voluntary departure on his behalf in September 2004. According to the respondent’s affidavit, dated April 29, 2005, Mr. Espinosa “never explained to me the consequences of requesting a voluntary departure compared to ¿theº immigration court issuing an order of deportation.” The respondent later made a motion to reopen, dated May 2, 2005, with which he included documentary evidence in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Upon consideration of the respondent’s submissions, we find that he has complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. However, the respondent has not demonstrated that the tactical decision made by former counsel to request voluntary departure caused him actual prejudice, for he has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for any form of relief from removal to warrant reopening of the proceedings. See Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98, 107 (3d Cir. 2005). Indeed, after the Third Circuit remanded this case to the Board, the parties were given an opportunity to file with the Board any further submissions by October 6, 2006. The respondent still has not demonstrated that he has an approved labor certification or approved visa petition; his current situation is no different from what it was when the Immigration Judge denied his motion to reopen more than 2 years ago. He cannot demonstrate eligibility for adjustment of status in accordance with section 245(a)(2) of the Act. See 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a)(2).
 
It is well-settled that motions to reopen are disfavored, and that a respondent who seeks to have the proceedings reopened must make a prima facie showing of eligibility for relief from removal. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992). See also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 107 (1988)( “There is a strong public interest in bringing litigation to a close as promptly as is consistent with the interest in giving the adversaries a fair opportunity to develop and present their respective cases”). As the respondent is already subject to an administratively final order of removal, he cannot have the proceedings reopened for an indefinite adjournment until some form of relief from removal becomes available to him. See Khan v. Atty. Gen. of U.S., 448 F.3d 226, 234-35 (3d Cir. 2006).
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 2, 2004, is dismissed. The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

At the time of the respondent’s initial motion to reopen, dated March 18, 2005, he asserted ineffective assistance of counsel, but failed to attach any evidence. On May 2, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to remand, with attached evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel. Although this evidence was submitted prior to the Board’s decision, it was not yet in the record at the time the Board issued the decision.

2007 WL 275803 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARYAM JAFAR SHIRLEY
FILE: AXX XX0 098 - LOUISVILLE
January 22, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles C. Nett, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Gordon
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION:
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated June 6, 2006. In our order, we adopted and affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge dated April 7, 2005, in which the Immigration Judge found that the respondent waived her right to apply for relief from removal by her failure to timely file her application for relief and supporting documentation. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion, which is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, will be denied.
 
The motion to reopen is untimely because it was originally filed on September 6, 20061 more than 90 days after our final order dated June 6, 2006. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, we decline to address the issue of equitable tolling in the respondent’s case because, even if the deadline was equitably tolled, she failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent did not file a bar complaint against her former counsel whom she asserts provided ineffective assistance, and her explanation as to why she did not file a complaint against her former counsel is inadequate. The respondent’s motion asserts numerous, significant legal errors by her former counsel, yet she states that she did not file a complaint against her former counsel because, in part, he forgave the balance on her account, represented her pro bono for 3 years, and did not “cover up his actions.” We empathize with the respondent’s situation, but the guidelines that we set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, were designed to protect the integrity of the legal system. In that regard, the requirement that disciplinary authorities be notified of breaches of professional conduct highlights the standards which should be expected of attorneys who represent persons in immigration proceedings, the outcome of which may, and often does, have enormous significance for the person. Id. at 639-40. A respondent cannot disregard the Lozada, supra, requirements simply because she does not want to file a bar complaint against her former counsel. To allow for such an exception would render moot the requirements that we have set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 605 (BIA 1996) (describing the reasoning behind the Lozada requirement for the filing of a bar complaint); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556-60 (BIA 2003) (noting the continued viability of the Lozada requirements). Based on the foregoing, we will deny the respondent’s motion to reopen because she did not comply with the requirements that we have set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2007 WL 275803 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2007 WL 275736 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUJAHID NASSEM CHATTHA
FILE: AXX XX7 697 - CHICAGO
January 22, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey A. Kriezelman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas O’Malley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGES:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(A)º - Excludable at entry under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i) º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of material fact
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated September 1, 2005, which denied the respondent’s request for waivers pursuant to section 241(a)(1)(H) and 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
In his motion, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not revisiting his deportability pursuant to section 241(a)(1)(A), and also erred in denying his requests for relief. We find a remand proper so that the respondent may be afforded the opportunity to present evidence regarding his deportability. In so finding, we will outline the procedural history of the respondent’s case to clarify the necessity of revisiting the respondent’s deportability.
 
The respondent was served an Order to Show Cause in July of 1994, and charged with section 241(a)(1)(A) of the Act, with underlying allegations that he was excludable at the time of his entry because, while he received a visa as the unmarried son of a lawful permanent resident, the DHS alleged that he was, in fact, married at the time (Exh. 1). The respondent received a merits hearing in 1995, represented by Elliot DuBois, and in a decision dated December 20, 1995, the Immigration Judge found the respondent deportable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent appealed this decision, and also filed a motion to remand based on allegedly unavailable evidence regarding his marital status, and alleged that his inability to file proper evidence regarding his marital status at the hearing was a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. This Board dismissed his appeal on April 25, 2001, although we granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure.1 In so finding, we also addressed the respondent’s motion to remand and found that he had failed to comply with the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Thereafter, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen, on June 26, 2001, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and submitted evidence that Mr. DuBois had been disbarred from practicing law during the period of time he represented the respondent. The DHS filed a nonopposition to this motion, indicating its belief that the respondent had attempted, in good faith, to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. On October 31, 2001, this Board granted the respondent’s motion based on its non-opposition by the DHS, and remanded the record to the Immigration Court.
 
*2 Most recently, the Immigration Judge conducted a hearing on the respondent’s requests for relief from deportation, but did not revisit the respondent’s deportability, in part, because our April 21, 2001, decision agreed with the Immigration Judge’s findings regarding the respondent’s fraudulent misrepresentations regarding his marital status (Tr. at 38). The Immigration Judge also noted that the Board’s October 31, 2001, remand order gave no directive regarding the issues to be addressed upon remand, and because the respondent’s June 26, 2001, motion referred only to the respondent’s requests for relief, the Immigration Judge chose to tailor the hearing to address only the respondent’s waiver requests (Tr. at 37-39).
 
While we understand the Immigration Judge’s confusion regarding the issues to be addressed upon remand, we note that the basis of the respondent’s Lozada motion, granted by this Board on October 31, 2001, indicates that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel during his initial deportation hearing in 1995. Mr. DuBois represented the respondent during his initial deportation hearing and throughout his appellate process. Inasmuch as the respondent did not receive effective counsel during his 1995 deportation hearing, it is incumbent upon the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent a full and fair hearing upon remand so that he may be afforded the proper due process. See Matter of Lozada supra. Thus, on remand, the Immigration Judge should conduct a full hearing regarding the respondent’s deportability, and if found deportable, any relief therefrom.2 As such, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

We note that, in his most recent appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in giving “diminished weight” to any equities subsequent to 1995 (2005 I.J. Dec. at 8). We agree, and recognize that the respondent’s ongoing proceedings are not frivolous.

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated July 26, 2006. This matter was previously before this Board on July 26, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s March 15, 2005, decision denying his application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) Of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). We also denied the respondent’s motion to reopen (which was filed concurrently with his appeal) based on ineffective assistance of counsel. On October 24, 2006, the respondent, through newly retained counsel, filed a second motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of the respondent’s two former counsels. Subsequently, on October 30, 2006, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) filed its opposition to the present motion to reopen. The respondent advances several contentions in his present motion, including that he did not receive a benefit as a consequence of his false representation of United States citizenship. See Memorandum of Points and Authorities in Support of Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 10. Following review of the voluminous evidence submitted in support of the respondent’s present motion, we find that the respondent failed to demonstrate prejudice which persuades us to disturb our decision dated July 26, 2006. In particular, we note that respondent’s new counsel has not presented evidence that, if previously presented, would have resulted in a positive exercise of discretion to the respondent, who has repeatedly misrepresented his immigration and citizenship status in order to procure employment and education benefits. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2007 WL 275801 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FENG QING LIN A.K.A. YAP PEK KUAN
FILE: AXX XX4 959 - NEW YORK
January 22, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Henry Zhang, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 19, 2005. The respondent failed to appear for her hearing on May 11, 2004. She was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. On May 18, 2004, a motion to reopen was filed based on a claim of no notice. The Immigration Judge denied the motion. More than a year later, on August 25, 2005, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. She claimed that her former attorney did not inform her of the hearing date, that he filed the prior motion without her consent, and that he made false statements in the motion and forged her signature on the false affidavit attached to the motion. She claims ineffective assistance of counsel. She states that she has complied with the requirements for such a claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
We find no merit in the respondent’s assertion in her 2005 motion that, due to a language barrier, she did not know the contents of the prior false motion until now. In her affidavit dated July 19, 2005, the respondent states that she was informed in June 2004 about the in absentia order and the prior motion filed by her former attorney, and that she was angry and took the papers to a few other lawyers who explained to her the false statements made by her former attorney in the motion and in the false affidavit. However, she waited until July 27, 2005, to file a complaint against her former attorney. The respondent has not demonstrated that she informed her former attorney of the allegations leveled against him or gave him an opportunity to respond. Although she filed a complaint with the ExeCutive Office of Imrnigration Review, she has not shown that she has filed a complaint against her former attorney with appropriate disciplinary authorities, such as the attorney’s state bar, with respect to any violation of his ethical or legal responsibilities. The respondent has not complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. She has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted. Her motion was appropriately denied. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

IN RE: GUSTAVO ARROYO-MORALES
FILE: AXX XX5 220 - CHICAGO
January 22, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Isuf Kola, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening, termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on August 25, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 22, 2006. In that decision the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable pursuant to sections 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), (B)(i) and (A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), (B)(i) and (A)(iii), as having been convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude; for having been convicted of a controlled substance violation; and as having been convicted of an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B), based on the respondent’s admissions (Tr. at 18-19) and record of convictions (Group Exh.2). Having found the respondent convicted of an aggravated felony under the Act, the Immigration Judge pretermitted his request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and ordered his removal to Mexico (I.J. at 3-4). See Ali v. Ashcroft, 395 F. 3d 722 (7th Cir. 2005). Furthermore, the Board denied a motion to remand filed by the respondent during the pendency of the appeal, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel by his former counsel, for failure to meet the requirements to establish such a claim as set out by the Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7 th Cir. 2001) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada). The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen seeking to terminate his proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent requests reopening on the grounds that the convictions which form the essential basis for the grounds of removal have now been vacated.
 
*2 The respondent has offered certified copies of 5 separate orders from the Circuit Court of the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit, Kane County, Illinois, dated October 2, 2006; October 4, 2006; October 18, 2006; and November 3, 2006, granting the respondent’s motion to vacate his pleas of guilty as to his January 27, 2006, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of Possession of Cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS; his November 10, 2005, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of Possession of Cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS; his October 13, 2005, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of Possession of Cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS; his November 14, 2003, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of Possession of Cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS; and his August 5, 2005, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of theft, in violation of section 5/16-1(a)(1) of chapter 720 of the ILCS, respectively, and vacating the respondent’s judgements of conviction entered in those cases. The record reflects that the basis for the granting of the respondent’s motions was “the failure to properly admonish the defendant” in violation of 725 ILCS 5/113-8. The only remaining conviction is a March 24, 2006, conviction in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, for the offense of Aggravated Battery/Public Place in violation of section 12-4(b)(8) of chapter 720 of the ILCS, which although a conviction for a crime involving moral turpitude, was committed more than 5 years after the respondent’s admission for lawful permanent residence in the United States. Moreover, no term of imprisonment was imposed, and as such, the respondent argues that conviction will not support the grounds of removal. We observe the respondent’s motion was properly served on the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), and the DHS has filed no opposition or other response to this motion, and the motion is deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3).
 
The question here is whether there still exists a conviction rendering the respondent removable from the United States, as charged; and if so, whether he can now establish his eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the proceedings should be terminated. Consequently, the motion will be granted and the record will be remanded to enable the Immigration Judge to consider the new evidence offered by the respondent relating to his removability pursuant to sections 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), (B)(i) and (A)(iii) of the Act, and to determine whether there presently remain any grounds for removal and whether he can now establish his eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the proceedings should be terminated. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is granted. Furthermore, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
*3 FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.

2007 WL 275828 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TIMOTHY PAUL NICOME
FILE: AXX XX4 186 - OAKDALE
January 18, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jin Li, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nora E. Norman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h)
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 28, 2006, and moves to remand for adjustment of status based upon ineffective assistance of his prior counsel. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent abandoned his application for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge gave the respondent a reasonable opportunity to file the required documentation and apply for adjustment of status, but he failed to do so timely. “If an application or document is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application or document shall be deemed waived.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). Because the respondent failed to file the required documents his adjustment application was deemed abandoned, and the Immigration Judge correctly ordered the respondent removed. See also Matter of Balibundi, 19 I&N Dec. 606 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent contends that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance of counsel in the failure to file timely the application for adjustment of status. This contention is foreclosed by a decision from the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit in Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605,609 (5th Cir. 2006) (alien has no right to effective assistance of counsel in requesting a discretionary waiver of removal from the Attorney General). “¿Tºhe failure to receive relief that is purely discretionary in nature does not amount to a deprivation of a liberty interest.” Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 475 (5th Cir. 2004) (citations omitted). As this case arises in the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, this Board follows circuit court precedent in cases arising in that circuit. Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25 (BIA 1989). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed, and the motion to remand is denied.

2007 WL 275812 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BASIL JAMES MUNROE
FILE: AXX XX1 687 - NEW YORK
January 18, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Theodore N. Cox, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lauren T. Farber
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; waiver of inadmissibility; remand
  
ORDER:
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has timely filed an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s May 31,2005, decision. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s 2001 conviction for criminal possession of a controlled substance (crack cocaine and marijuana) constituted a removable offense under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), ordered him removed from the United States, and found that no relief from removal was available, albeit none was requested. On appeal and in a motion to reopen, through counsel, the respondent asserts that based on his previous counsel’s ineffective assistance he wrongfully conceded to the charge of removability and failed to apply for any applications for relief. The respondent seeks a remand to the Immigration Judge in order to apply for such relief. The respondent’s request for oral argument before this Board is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2006). The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
As for counsel’s decision not to file any requests for relief and conceding the charge of removability on the respondent’s behalf, we would observe that absent a showing of egregious circumstances rising to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel, decisions of counsel are binding on the respondent. Cf. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986).1 To the extent that the respondent argues that prior counsel’s representation was constitutionally ineffective, we find that he has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); however, he has not provided persuasive evidence of prejudice arising from counsel’s allegedly deficient performance, i.e., evidence reflecting a reasonable possibility that the outcome of his removal hearing would have been different had counsel not declined to apply for relief on his behalf or conceded the charge of removability. See, e.g., Waldron v. INS, 17 F.3d 511 (2d Cir. 1993). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is removable as charged based on his 2001 conviction under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.03 for criminal possession of a controlled substance (crack cocaine and marijuana) in the 7th degree (Exh. 2, Tab 3), as this crime is a violation “relating to” a controlled substance under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II). In his brief on appeal, the respondent erroneously refers to his 2001 conviction as one for “marijuana possession” only—however, the record of conviction reflects that he was in possession of both marijuana and crack cocaine (see Exh. 2, Tab 3).
 
*2 Respondent contends that he should have been eligible for a number of waivers of inadmissibility for his various convictions in the past, including former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996), sections 212(h) and (i) of the Act, as well as cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). We find his arguments to be unpersuasive.
 
Relief under former section 212(c) of the Act is not available with respect to convictions arising on or after April 1, 1997. 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(3). The respondent’s 2001 conviction occurred after the delimiting date of eligibility for 212(c) relief. Furthermore, relief under the provisions of section 212(h) of the Act is unavailable because that provision only applies “insofar as it relates to a single offense of simple possession of 30 grams or less of marijuana.…” Section 212(h) of the Act. The waiver can only be applied to one conviction and the respondent has more than one conviction for marijuana possession (Exhs. 2 and 3). Regarding relief under section 212(i), we find no applicability of this section to any of the respondent’s convictions.
 
Lastly, cancellation of removal is not available in this case for two reasons. First, the respondent does not meet the continuous residence requirements under section 240A(a) of the Act. In order for a lawful permanent resident alien to be eligible for cancellation of removal, he or she must have resided continuously in the United States for a period of 7 years after having been admitted in any status. Section 240A(a)(2) of the Act. The respondent first entered the United States as a lawful permanent resident in December 1974 (Exh. 2, Tab 1). His physical presence ended on July 18, 1979, the date of commission of the first qualifying offense. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act; Matter of Perez, 22 I&N Dec. 689, 692-93 (BIA 1999) (indicating that continuous residence or physical presence for cancellation of removal purposes is deemed to end on the date that a qualifying offense has been committed). As this offense was committed less than 5 years after his December 1974 admission, the respondent is ineligible for cancellation of removal.
 
The respondent is also ineligible for cancellation of removal on the basis of his conviction for an aggravated felony. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. In addition to the July 1979 and November 2001 controlled substance convictions, the respondent was also convicted in August 1990 under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.03 for criminal possession of a controlled substance in the 7th degree (Exh. 3, Tab 3). Both the 1990 and 2001 convictions constitute a drug-trafficking aggravated felony under sections 101(a)(43)(B) and 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(43)(B) and 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii). The term aggravated felony is defined by section 101 (a)(43)(B) of the Act to include “illicit trafficking in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act), including a drug trafficking crime (as defined in section 924(c) of title 18; United States Code).” The respondent’s offenses, which involved possession of controlled substances, do not constitute “illicit trafficking” offenses. See Matter of Davis, 20 I&N Dec. 536 (BIA 1992), modified, Matter of Yanez, 23 I&N Dec. 390 (BIA 2002). Accordingly, those offenses can qualify as aggravated felonies only under the “drug trafficking crime” clause of section 101 (a)(43)(B). In removal proceedings, the status of an offense as a drug trafficking crime is determined by reference to the decisional law of the controlling circuit court of appeals, and not by reference to any independent standard articulated by this Board. Matter of Yanez, supra.
 
*3 The Supreme Court recently, in Lopez v. Gonzales, ____ U.S. ____ (No. 05-547, decided December 5, 2006), noted that “recidivist possession” of drag offenses constitute “drug trafficking crimes” because they are punishable as felonies under the Controlled Substances Act. See Id. at footnote 6. In addition, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, has held that a state conviction for simple possession of a controlled substance qualifies as a drug trafficking crime under 18 U.S.C. § 924(c) and, by extension, an aggravated felony, if the offense in question was committed after a prior controlled substance conviction became final. United States v. Simpson, 319 F.3d 81 (2d Cir. 2002) (citing 21 U.S.C. § 844(a)). Both of the respondent’s August 1990 and November 2001 convictions for seventh-degree criminal possession of a controlled substance occurred after the respondent’s 1979 conviction for a similar offense became final. Therefore, Second Circuit law dictates that his 1990 and 2001 convictions were for a drug trafficking crime under 18 U.S.C. § 924(c) and an aggravated felony under section 101 (a)(43)(B). Because either of the respondent’s 1990 or 2001 offenses qualifies as an aggravated felony under Second Circuit law, the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act and statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent alleges that a hearing disability, which appears to have arisen towards the end of the proceedings, contributed to his denial of due process. Even if true, we find that the respondent was not prejudiced in any way by his alleged hearing disability because the documentary evidence supports the Immigration Judge’s findings.

2007 WL 275767 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICENTE HERNANDEZ
FILE: AXX XX5 198 - SALT LAKE CITY
January 17, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. Christopher Keen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Matthew R. Hall,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on September 28, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. On October 30, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen1 alleging ineffective assistance by former counsel. The motion is denied.
 
In his affidavit submitted with the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, the respondent averred that he was aware of the hearing date but did not appear because “I thought that the Master Hearing was going to be continued” and “I am not sure if there was a miscommunication between me and the Law Firm or if I just misunderstood what I had been told by my lawyer or by his staff-persons.” In the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, former counsel, Mr. Lofgren, stated that although his firm had failed to remind the respondent the night before the hearing, his co-counsel “was fully under the impression that the Respondent was going to appear.” Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, page 2. Former counsel also maintained in the motion that he believed there was “some sort of miscommunication or misunderstanding between this Firm and the Respondent” that caused the respondent to believe that the hearing would be continued. Id.
 
In the current motion before the Board, the respondent now swears in the attached affidavit that former counsel told the respondent that he did not have to attend his hearing on December 20, 2005, because it would be continued. Former counsel’s statement, submitted with the current motion, indicates that his office normally calls clients to remind them of hearings, but in this case they failed to do so. In addition, former counsel states that the respondent did not appear for his hearing because counsel’s office told the respondent that his appeal of the CIS denial of his adjustment application would stop the instant removal proceedings.
 
We agree with the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) that the current affidavits are not consistent with what the respondent averred in the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. While the previous motion papers indicate possible confusion and misunderstanding, the respondent now contends that former counsel explicitly told him he did not have to attend his hearing. Moreover, we agree with DHS counsel that pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), the respondent was required to attest in detail to the agreement with former counsel as to what actions the counsel would be making on the respondent’s behalf at the hearing below. Despite the Board previously noting in our September 28, 2006, decision that the respondent had failed to fulfill this requirement, no such attestation has yet been proffered. See Tang v. Ashcroft, 354 F.3d 1192, 1196 (10th Cir. 2003)(in order to assert an ineffective assistance claim in support of a motion to reopen, alien must comply with the Board’s Lozada requirements).
 
*2 The procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are essentially a “screening device” which the Board considers before considering the merits of ineffective assistance claims. Infanzon v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 1359, 1362 (10th Cir.2004). Here, the record does not reveal a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel. Instead, the respondent has not alleged, let alone established, that former counsel ever agreed to remind the respondent of his hearing.2 The respondent concedes, and the record reflects, that he received notice of the hearing (Exh. 2). In addition, we note that the record does not contain a Form EOIR-28, Notice of Entry of Appearance before Immigration Court, from Mr. Lofgren. Given the inconsistent accounts and lack of any details regarding the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken during proceedings below, we cannot find that the respondent failed to appear due to ineffective assistance by former counsel.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the motion is denied.

No evidence has been proffered that indicates there was any such agreement or understanding that the respondent would be reminded of the hearing. 2007 WL 275778 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO JORGE CARDOSO
FILE: AXX XX9 836 - OAKDALE
January 16, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This matter was last before us on September 12, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision ordering him removed to his native Cape Verde as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. In our decision, we observed that the respondent had conceded his removability through counsel, and held that this concession qualified as a binding judicial admission. The respondent has now filed a motion, styled as a “motion to reconsider,” in which he claims that his prior attorney’s concession of removability constituted ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
By statute and regulation, a motion to reconsider must state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in a prior Board decision, and it must be supported by pertinent authority. Section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C) (West 2005); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (2006). Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 56-57 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s motion does not specify any legal or factual error in our prior decision upholding the Immigration Judge’s order of removal,1 but instead challenges the validity (or the wisdom) of the concession of removability upon which that order was based. As such, the motion is in the nature of a request for reopening on the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
To support a request for reopening on the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien in removal proceedings must (1) provide an affidavit attesting the relevant facts, including a statement of the terms of the attorney-client agreement; (2) inform counsel of the allegations and allow counsel an opportunity to respond; (3) file or explain why a grievance has not been filed against the offending attorney. Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 2006 WL 3616557, *2 (5th Cir., December 13, 2006) (citing Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 639 (BIA 1988)). Although we are mindful of the respondent’s pro se status, his motion must be denied because it does not satisfy any of these threshold requirements. As we observed in our prior decision, moreover, there are many valid reasons why counsel may concede a charge of removability, and therefore absent compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, we are unwilling to conclude that such a concession constitutes ineffective assistance on its face.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Although the motion reiterates the respondent’s prior appellate arguments regarding the validity of the charge of removability, the respondent does not deny that his counsel conceded removability on his behalf or that such a concession generally qualifies as a binding judicial admission.
