2007 WL 275760 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA CEBALLOS DE ZAVALA
FILE: AXX XX6 893 - IMPERIAL
January 11, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kimberly S. Greer, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on May 5, 2005, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s December 10, 2003, decision ordering the respondent removed to Mexico. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen which will be denied.
 
The respondent alleges that reopening is warranted, despite the untimeliness of her motion, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2006), because she was subject to ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal and that this, in turn, affected her ability to appeal to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. See, e.g., Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (discussing equitable tolling for untimely motions to reopen where an alien received ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent asserts that she has complied with the procedural requirements for asserting such a claim pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999) (stating that in order to prevail on a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish and include the following: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not). However, the respondent also argues that strict compliance with Matter of Lozada is not required inasmuch as her former attorney’s ineffective assistance of counsel is plain on its face. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
We find that the respondent has established a claim for equitable tolling, but nevertheless decline to reopen. While the majority of the cases cited by the respondent in her brief (Respondent’s Br. at 8-9) pertain to situations where counsel has deceived an alien or committed fraud,1 which is not the claim here, the Ninth Circuit has held that equitable tolling may apply whenever a party is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of a claim. Socop-Gonzales v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (citations omitted). “¿Tºhe party invoking tolling need only show that his or her ignorance of the limitations period was caused by circumstances beyond the party’s control.” Id (citations omitted). Here, the respondent alleges that she was not aware that her previous counsel failed to raise certain issues on appeal — believing they had been preserved — until the Ninth Circuit dismissed her appeal for failing to exhaust administrative remedies available to her. Assuming this failure prejudiced her, the respondent’s former counsel’s conduct could qualify as ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that it did not and decline to reopen or remand.
 
*2 First, the respondent did not comply, substantially or wholly, with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada. Although the respondent has provided a declaration about her agreement with her former counsel, both her letter to counsel informing him of the nature of her allegations of misconduct, and her complaint to the state bar, allege misconduct based on counsel’s failure to file a timely appeal. However, as the respondent’s former counsel explains in his response, we never found the appeal untimely. In other words, the respondent does not inform her previous counsel or the state authorities about the true nature of her allegations of misconduct — that former counsel failed adequately to appeal the issue of whether evidence should have been suppressed below and whether the respondent was removable as charged.
 
The respondent is correct that the Ninth Circuit does not always require strict compliance with the procedural requirements we have set forth in Lozada (Respondent’s Br. at 5). See Rodriguez-Lariz, supra; Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). However, the fact that the respondent has failed to meet the Lozada requirements precludes any presumption that the respondent was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra at 526 (stating that the Lozada factors are intended to ensure that an adequate factual record exists for an ineffective assistance of counsel complaint and that the complaint is legitimate). Therefore, she has failed to meet the threshold requirements for reopening based on such a claim.
 
Furthermore, we disagree that the alleged misconduct is evident on the face of the record, as alleged by the respondent, such that compliance with the procedural requirements are not necessary (Respondent’s Br. at 5). Unlike other cases where the Ninth Circuit has found obvious cases of ineffective assistance of counsel and, therefore, no need to comply with Lozada, the respondent has failed to establish that her previous attorney’s decision about what issues to raise on appeal and how to articulate them were anything but strategy or, at the most, inadvertence. There certainly is no indication that the respondent’s former counsel lied to the respondent, engaged in fraud, filed the wrong type of petition, or failed to appear. See Rodriguez-Lariz, supra, at 1224-25. Ineffective assistance of counsel in a deportation hearing results in a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment only when the proceeding is so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting her case. Id. at 1226. The respondent’s former counsel raised the issue of suppression of evidence on appeal — which relates to whether the DHS established the respondent’s removability. It is true that the respondent’s former counsel only raised one of the Immigration Judge’s reasons for finding that the evidence should not be suppressed and that, consequently, the Ninth Circuit held that the respondent failed to exhaust her administrative remedies because the alternative finding was not appealed. However, strategies which are unwise in hindsight do not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Gonzalez v. INS, 640 F.2d 1139, 1142 (9th Cir. 1981). Furthermore, to establish prejudice, “¿tºhe defendant must show that there is a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s unprofessional errors, the result of the proceeding would have been different. A reasonable probability is a probability sufficient to undermine confidence in the outcome.” Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 694 (1984). As on appeal, the respondent has failed to persuade us that there was error in the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the evidence was not tainted and that it established that the respondent was removable for having smuggled in an alien.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

See Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000); Lopez v. INS, supra; see also Rodriguez-Lariz, supra.

2007 WL 275724 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NASEEM, ASMA
FILE: AXX-XX6-425 - NEW YORK
January 11, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ruchi Thaker, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated May 24, 2005. The respondents have raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and maintain that they have sufficiently complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). It appears, however, that in an effort to comply with Lozada’s requirement that current counsel alert prior counsel to the charges against him and allow him the opportunity to respond, counsel simply sent prior counsel a copy of the motion on the same day that the motion was filed. It would be better practice for counsel to do former counsel and the Board the courtesy of providing former counsel an opportunity to respond to the charges prior to filing a Lozada motion. Counsel should endeavor to ascertain the merits of a respondent’s claims before filing in order to avoid potentially besmirching prior counsel’s reputation wrongly and occupying the Board’s time with a frivolous motion.
 
Because prior counsel has vigorously denied the accusations leveled against him in the respondents’ motion, we find it appropriate to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Court for the sole initial purpose of allowing the Immigration Judge and the parties to address in the first instance the question as to whether prior counsel did, in fact, provide ineffective assistance to the respondents at certain critical points in their proceedings, as claimed. If, after resolving the necessary factual and legal arguments, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondents’ claims as to ineffective assistance are without merit, the Immigration Judge should once again enter orders of removal based upon the respondents’ failure to appear for their May 13, 2004, hearing. If, however, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondents’ claims as to ineffective assistance have merit, the Immigration Judge should then entertain any applications for relief from removal. Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2007 WL 275851 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAREK TOBIASZ A.K.A. MAREK ADAM TOBIASZ
FILE: AXX XX0 607 - NEW YORK CITY
January 10, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael P. DiRaimondo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James D. Paoli
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on April 5, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision, which had denied his suspension of deportation application and granted him voluntary departure. The respondent, through new counsel, now has filed a motion to reopen, which will be denied.
 
On April 5, 2002, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision which had denied his suspension application. We agreed with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent had not established that his deportation to Poland would cause extreme hardship to himself or his United States citizen son, and we noted that the respondent’s counsel had not filed a brief as indicated. We granted the respondent 30 days in which to voluntarily depart the United States. The respondent has never departed the United States as instructed, and he remains in the United States illegally.
 
As a preliminary matter, we observe that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. Our prior order in these proceedings was entered on April 5, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2006), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before July 7, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until more than 4 years later, on October 24, 2006. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) argues in its response that the motion should be denied solely on this basis.
 
Although the respondent’s motion is based in part on the alleged ineffective assistance of his former counsel, who has faced state bar disciplinary action for wholly unrelated conduct, the respondent does not allege sufficient facts that would compel us to apply the principle of “equitable tolling” to the motion filing deadline. Specifically, he has alleged no misconduct on the part of former counsel that prevented him from filing his motion in a timely manner, or at least somewhat sooner than it has been filed.1
 
Therefore he has failed to exercise due diligence in his case to obtain equitable tolling under applicable Second Circuit law.2 Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000)(alien who waited 2 1/2 years to file motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel did not establish due diligence to merit equitable tolling). Moreover, we note that the respondent’s counsel has attached copies of two previous unpublished Board orders in which he filed motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of previous counsel on behalf of other aliens, and he alleges that the Board is “obligated to follow its prior decisions.” In this regard, we point out that unpublished Board orders do not represent binding precedent in the respondent’s case. Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216 (BIA 1990); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(g).
 
*2 We observe that previous counsel’s failure to file a brief was not the only reason we cited in our prior order as the sole basis for dismissing the appeal, as incorrectly alleged by present counsel in his motion. Our order dismissing the respondent’s appeal indicated that we had reviewed the Immigration Judge’s decision, and agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had failed to establish extreme hardship to either himself or his United States citizen son to qualify for suspension. Under these circumstances, as the respondent has not established prejudice, he has not met the standards for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent also has alleged that his motion deserves to be granted in the exercise of discretion because of new facts, including the maturing and educational advancement of his now 13-year-old son, and the respondent’s own marriage to a lawful permanent resident. We decline to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen the respondent’s case on this basis. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2

Although counsel includes an unexplained citation to Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108 (2d Cir. 1993) in a supplemental letter, we observe that the alien’s counsel in Esposito v. INS, supra, failed to file an appeal with the Board. Therefore, the issue of an attorney’s failure to file a brief on appeal was not addressed, and that case may be distinguished on the facts from the instant case.

2007 WL 129752 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CORINA VITERUO-ACUNA
FILE: AXX XX4 142 - NEWARK
January 5, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yvette W. Lewis-Williams, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent is a citizen of Peru. On July 20, 1994, she appeared before an Immigration Judge, and agreed to voluntarily depart the United States by October 24, 1994. That decision was not appealed to the Board. On May 3, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. with the Immigration Judge. Therein, the respondent requested reopening to seek adjustment of status as a victim of abuse by her United States citizen spouse.1 The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely on June 12, 2006, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be sustained.
 
Section 1003.23(b)(4) of 8 Code of Federal Regulations provides that “except as provided in paragraph (b)(4)(ii) of this section, a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.”
 
The record reflects that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed with the Immigration Judge on May 3, 2006. Because the respondent’s motion was filed after September 30, 1996, it is untimely and was properly denied by the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4); Da Cruz v. INS, 4 F.3d 721 (9th Cir. 1993). We have also determined that the exceptions to these filing requirements set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) are inapplicable.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she did not understand the nature and consequences of her deportation hearing, and therefore that decision is not final. However, the period for appealing, reopening, and reconsidering the Immigration Judge’s decision has elapsed. The decision of the Immigration Judge rendered on July 20, 1994, is final. This aspect of the appeal is dismissed.
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that she is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for presenting such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98 (3rd Cir. 2005). We therefore cannot reopen these proceedings on account of this reason.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that her motion should have been deemed a joint motion to reopen. She argues that the motion was unopposed because the DHS adjudication officer recommended that she seek reopening before the Immigration Judge, and never attempted to execute the deportation order. The respondent further notes that the DHS may have filed its opposition to her request for reopening untimely. Notwithstanding her contentions, we cannot find that any recommendation that the respondent may have received constitutes a non-opposition to her motion provided to the Immigration Court. In addition, even if the DHS filed an untimely opposition to her motion to reopen, we cannot find that such constitutes a joint request for reopening. We therefore affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely filed.
 
*2 The Board’s discretion to reopen sua sponte is limited to cases where exceptional situations are demonstrated. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We note that the respondent is apparently the victim of domestic abuse, as she is the beneficiary of an approved Form I-360. Moreover, the respondent married prior to the commencement of proceedings, and has been in this country for approximately 16 years. She is also the parent of a 7-year-old United States citizen child, and has been under an order of supervision with the DHS since March 2006. Having considered the totality of the circumstances, we find that an exceptional situation exists to reopen these proceedings sua sponte.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The DHS filed its opposition to the motion on May 16, 2006.

2006 WL 3922243 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE ARMANDO SILVA-GARZA
FILE: A41 768 886 - HOUSTON
December 29, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan J. Mendoza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility; remand
 
The respondent has appealed the November 1, 2005, and December 12, 2005,1 decisions of an Immigration Judge which found him removable, pretermitted his application for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), and ordered him removed to Mexico. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The DHS opposes the appeal and the motion. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
In a Notice to Appear dated November 4, 2004, it was alleged that: the respondent’s status was adjusted to that of a lawful permanent resident on April 3, 1991, and that he was convicted in Texas on February 7, 1992, for burglary of a building with intent to commit theft, and on April 29, 1996, for tampering with a government record. The respondent was charged with removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), as an alien convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of criminal misconduct. The respondent indicated that he wanted to pursue a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(c) of the Act.
 
At a hearing on November 1, 2005, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) asserted that the respondent was not eligible for a 212(c) waiver under section 440(d) of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act (AEDPA) due his removability for two crimes involving moral turpitude, one of which involved a plea made after the enactment of AEDPA. The Immigration Judge then entered a summary order finding the respondent removable as charged based on his concessions, and gave the DHS until December 1, 2005, to file a motion to pretermit. The respondent was given until December 8, 2005, to reply.
 
On November 2, 2005, the DHS filed a motion to pretermit based on the argument it presented at the hearing. It also submitted a Form I-261 which lodged an additional charge of removability under section 237(a)(1)(A)(i) of the Act, for a conviction for a crime involving moral turpitude (CIMT) committed within 5 years of entry for which a sentence of 1 year or longer may be imposed. The DHS also made the following substitute allegations of fact: that the respondent was admitted as a conditional resident on December 15, 1988, and that he was convicted of burglary on February 2, 1992, for a crime committed on January 12, 1992. The DHS added the following allegations of fact: that the conditional nature of the respondent’s status was removed on April 3, 1991, and that a year or longer could have been imposed for his burglary conviction. On December 5, 2005, the respondent filed additional documents to support his 212(c) application, but he did not respond to the motion to pretermit.
 
*2 On December 12, 2005, the Immigration Judge issued an order granting the motion to pretermit, and ordering the respondent removed to Mexico. Her reasoning was that the respondent failed to respond to the DHS’ motion, and therefore it was deemed unopposed.
 
The respondent subsequently filed this appeal. He asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in granting the DHS’ motion to pretermit solely on the lack of any response, and that she should have given him an opportunity to respond to the allegations and charge in the Form I-261. He also argues that the DHS failed to meet its burden of showing that his conviction for tampering with a government record was a CIMT. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. He argues that his counsel at the hearing erred in failing to respond to the DHS’ motion to pretermit, and in not advising him to apply for cancellation of removal for permanent residents. He has filed supporting documents to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The DHS opposes the appeal and the motion.
 
We are remanding the record for the following reasons. The Immigration Judge’s summary finding of removability made on November 1, 2005, refers to a concession of removability. However, there is no concession of removability in the record, and no explanation as to why both of the respondent’s crimes were deemed to be CIMTs (see Tr. at 7-8; 12-13). This alone is a basis for a remand. Further, the Immigration Judge should not have entered her final order without addressing the lodged allegations and charge filed by the DHS. Further, the respondent should have been given the opportunity to respond to the allegations and additional charge. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.30, 1240.10(e).
 
Finally, we question the Immigration Judge’s decision to grant the motion to pretermit without providing any reasoning except that it was unopposed. The underlying issue involved a legal determination which should have been specifically addressed. Nonetheless, the DHS appears correct because in the case of a guilty plea made between April 24, 1996, and April 1, 1997, an application for a 212(c) waiver is subject to the limitations of AEDPA § 440(d). See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(2). Section 440(d) of AEDPA changed the last sentence of section 212(c) of the Act to provide that:
This subsection shall not apply to an alien who is deportable by reason of having committed any criminal offense covered in section 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), (B),(C), or (D), or any offense covered by section 241(a)(2)(A)(ii) ¿two CIMTsº for which both predicate offenses are, without regard to the date of their commission, otherwise covered by section 241(a)(2)(A)(i).
 
*3 See also Matter of Fuentes-Campos, 21 I&N Dec. 905, 906 (BIA 1997). The issue is therefore whether the respondent’s individual convictions for a CIMT fall under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act (formerly section 241(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act).2 The CIMT charges therefore need to be premised on a conviction for which a sentence of 1 year or longer may be imposed. In this regard, the lodged allegations of fact become important, and the underlying statutes and conviction records must be examined to determine the nature of the respondent’s convictions. It appears that both convictions were for felonies (see Exh. 2, attachments), but this is a matter which is more appropriately resolved on remand.3 See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (precluding the Board from engaging in fact finding on appeal). Insofar as the respondent contends in his motion that he is eligible for cancellation of removal, his 1992 crime cuts off the necessary period of continuous physical presence if calculated from his entry as a conditional permanent resident in 1988. See sections 240A(a)(2) and 240(d)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(2) and (d)(1)(B); Matter of Jurado, 23 I&N Dec. 29, 32 (BIA 2006). Because the record is being remanded for a new hearing, we need not address the respondent’s allegations that he had ineffective assistance of counsel at the earlier proceedings. An appropriate order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge dated the latter decision August 12, 2005. The transmittal letter attached to the decision, and other documents in the record, indicate this is the wrong date.

2

Section 241 of the Act applied to deportation proceedings, and was the precursor to section 237 of the Act. The regulations state that § 440(d) applies to aliens in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(2). We note that prior to the enactment of AEDPA, the single CIMT ground required that an alien be sentenced to confinement for 1 year or longer for his or her crime. This version of the ground only applies to aliens whose proceedings were initiated prior to AEDPA’s enactment date of April 24, 1996. See Matter of Fortiz, 21 I&N Dec. 1199, 1202-03 (BIA 1998).

3

On appeal, the respondent asserts that he is not subject to § 440(d) limitations because his second crime was committed more than five years after his admission. However, the language of § 440(d) provides that the evaluation of the two CIMTs shall be made “regardless of the date of their commission.”


2006 WL 3922243 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3922306 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MORVIS SICKARNEY GOODE A.K.A. MARVIS GOODE
FILE: AXX XX4 392 - FISHKILL
December 26, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 26, 2006. On August 23, 2006, the respondent moved for reopening so that he could apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, to waive the immigration consequences of his 1996 conviction for attempted sale of cocaine. Exh. 1. The respondent also argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his original removal hearing (an order of removal was issued by an Immigration Judge on March 18, 2002) because his former counsel failed to file an appeal. Insofar as the respondent argues that filing deadlines governing his motion to reopen should be tolled due to the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that the respondent’s motion makes no showing that he acted with due diligence over the intervening years to discover and remedy any ineffective assistance of his former counsel. Thus, equitable tolling cannot be applied to suspend the general filing deadlines that require that motions to reopen he filed within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(c); see Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). Moreover, we have stated that the mere failure to file an appeal does not necessarily equate with prejudicial ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (failure to timely file an appeal is not per se ineffective assistance of counsel without showing that alien would likely have prevailed on appeal). Furthermore, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent’s motion is also untimely under the regulations governing special motions to reopen to apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(h) (requiring such motions to be filed by April 26, 2005). As the respondent has not met his burden to show that he is eligible for any relief from removal (particularly in view of the fact that by now, he appears to have already served more than five years, combined, for two aggravated felony convictions), there is no basis for reopening even if the motion were timely filed. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3922304 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REYNALDO IBARRA CASAREZ
FILE: AXX XX5 476 - COUNCIL BLUFFS
December 26, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on June 8, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. On July 6, 2006, the respondent filed an untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We will construe the respondent’s request as a motion to reopen sua sponte because his conviction, which was the basis for removability, was vacated and the underlying complaint dismissed for failure to prosecute.
 
The respondent has presented evidence that his conviction was vacated based on allegations that his plea violated the Iowa Rules of Criminal Procedure, he did not waive his right to make a motion and arrest of judgment, and his plea was inadequate due to no factual basis, ineffective assistance of counsel, and failure to understand the elements of the crime. In addition, the respondent presented a certified copy of a court document indicating that the charges were dismissed due to lack of prosecution. Accordingly, the respondent’s conviction is no longer valid for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006)(a conviction vacated because trial court failed to advise alien defendant of immigration consequences of a guilty plea is no longer a valid conviction for immigration purposes); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). Since the reason for finding that the respondent was removable no longer exists, the respondent has demonstrated exceptional circumstances justifying sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board retains limited discretionary power under the regulations to reopen or reconsider only in exceptional circumstances). Accordingly, the motion to reopen sua sponte is granted, the prior decisions of the Board and the Immigration Judge are vacated, and the record remanded for further proceedings.

2006 WL 3922266 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISMAIL, YOUSSEF
FILE: AXX-XX3-764 - NEWARK
December 22, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thaker, Ruchi, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated March 10, 2004. At that time, we denied the respondent’s appeal as untimely filed. More than 2 years later, on November 6, 2006, the respondent submitted a motion to accept his appeal by certification due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, Joseph C. Sekula. He claimed that he consulted with various lawyers over the last 2 years about what, if anything, could be done on his case. He stated that he retained a lawyer who obtained his immigration record on August 29, 2006, he then retained a different lawyer after he was detained on August 30, 2006, and finally he retained another lawyer after that. He offers a copy of a letter addressed to the Office of Attorney Ethics, dated November 3, 2006, regarding Mr. Sekula. It includes a notation that a copy will be sent to Mr. Sekula. However, there is no evidence that the complaint was filed, and no evidence that Mr. Sekula was actually informed of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond. We find that the respondent has not presented a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, there is no evidence that the respondent took any action regarding the alleged ineffective assistance of Mr. Sekula until late in 2006. His lack of diligence indicates that reopening is not warranted as a matter of discretion. We find no basis to consider the respondent’s appeal on certification pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2006 WL 3922153 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED H.EL HEFNAWY
FILE: AXX XX3 762 - PHILADELPHIA
December 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David E. Piver, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated May 5, 2006. In our prior decision, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent now argues that the motion should have been granted due to the ineffectiveness of his former counsel and that the record should be remanded to permit him to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) does not oppose the motion, which will be denied.
 
We note that the respondent alleges that his appeal was untimely due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he argues that his prior counsel failed to timely file a motion to reopen the Immigration Judge’s decision or to notify the respondent that he had failed to do so. As a result, the respondent’s voluntary departure period was not tolled and the respondent failed to leave at the end of that period, thereby rendering him ineligible for certain forms of relief for 10 years.
 
Even if we were to find that the respondent has met the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel in this case, see Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003), the respondent has not met the requirements for a motion to reopen for consideration of his adjustment of status application. In his motion, the respondent alleges that the visa petition filed on his behalf by his lawful permanent resident spouse is pending for adjudication with the DHS but has not yet been approved. Further, although the respondent’s spouse has applied for her United States citizenship, she is not yet a United States citizen and the respondent’s priority date is not yet current. Therefore, even upon approval of the respondent’s petition, there is not a visa immediately available to him. Accordingly, he is not prima facie eligible for adjustment of status. See, e.g., INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (an alien must make a prima facie showing both statutory eligibility for the relief sought and that relief is warranted in the exercise of discretion); Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996) (an applicant demonstrates prima facie eligibility for relief where the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 3922159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAYRA MORALES
FILE: AXX XX1 397 - SAN DIEGO
December 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Zaehringer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathryn E. Stuever
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
By an order dated August 23, 2006, an Immigration Judge found the respondent to have abandoned any and all claims for removal relief, and ordered her removal from the United States. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision, as well as a motion to remand. The respondent’s request for a waiver of the filing fee associated with the filing of an appeal is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) and 1003.8(c). The motion will be granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that her immigration proceedings should be reopened due to the ineffective assistance of her prior attorney, Mr. Harpreet S. Brar. More specifically, the respondent avers that but for the actions of Mr. Brar, she would not only have contested her single charge of removability, but she would also have submitted an alternative motion to withdraw her application for admission within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge. The respondent further argues that Mr. Brat failed to inform her of the Immigration Court’s decision, which hampered her ability to file the instant appeal.
 
Before making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the alien must ordinarily comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Those requirements include: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved alien setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel, (2) that the representative whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the respondent’s motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. See id.; see also Siong v. INS, 376 F.3d 1030, 1036-37 (9th Cir. 2004). Moreover, the alien must also show prejudice from counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005); Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950, 955 (9th Cir.2003). Prejudice results when “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Munoz v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
*2 Based upon the record before us, we find that the respondent has substantially complied with the Lozada requirements. See Respondent’s Brief, sub-exhs. 1-3. We further find that the record supports the respondent’s contention that she was prejudiced by the actions of her former counsel. For instance, Mr. Brar failed to meet any of the filing requirements set by the Immigration Judge, despite his written acknowledgment of those deadlines. See Exh. 5 (evidencing counsel’s awareness that he was to submit both the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings and her withdrawal application for admission by July 20, 2006); see also Tr. at 10-17. See generally Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 588 n.5 (9th Cir. 2006); Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding ineffective assistance where counsel failed to file an application for relief for which the alien was prima facie eligible). The respondent’s prior attorney also failed to keep his client informed as to the status of her case. See generally Ray v. Gonzales, supra. Given the fact that she has been detained throughout these proceedings, we find that it was particularly incumbent upon Mr. Brar to keep in contact with the respondent. See generally Siong v. INS, supra. Thus, inasmuch as it appears that Mr. Brar’s failure to act denied the respondent an opportunity to establish her eligibility for any form of removal relief, we find that the respondent has suffered actual prejudice. Accordingly, we shall grant the respondent’s motion to reopen, and remand the record to the Immigration Court for additional proceedings.
 
In view of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2006 WL 3922166 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BORALDA ILIAS
FILE: AXX XX4 416 - DETROIT
December 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marshal E. Hyman, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on January 28, 2005, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal. On July 24, 2006, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen. The respondent contends that the time limitation on a motion to reopen, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), should be tolled and that her proceeding should be reopened due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003). Further, the respondent has shown ineffective assistance by her former counsel. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). The respondent avers that her former counsel specifically directed that she not appear at her initial master calendar hearing because he was seeking consolidation of her proceeding with that of her parents. The record contains a statement from the Department of Homeland Security anticipating that the respondent’s proceeding would be heard with her family (Exh. 3). However, the respondent’s former counsel never filed a motion to consolidate, and the record does not contain any other document indicating that a request to consolidate and/or transfer venue was filed or presented. In a motion to reopen/rescind filed with the Immigration Court, the respondent’s former counsel alleged that government counsel indicated that she would route the case so that it would be consolidated, but government counsel denied that she was the trial attorney at the respondent’s family’s master calendar hearings.
 
Given these facts and circumstances, we find that the actions and advice of the respondent’s former counsel constituted ineffective assistance and that the respondent has demonstrated due diligence in presenting her claim in this regard. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted. Further, our decision, as well as the Immigration Judge’s decisions, are vacated, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

2006 WL 3922226 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIUSEPPE AMOROSO A.K.A. JOE GUIDO
FILE: A18 850 801 - PHILADELPHIA
December 18, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas A. Grannan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of an aggravated felony
Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of a controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This matter has a long history before this Board, beginning with the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 1998, decision in which he found the respondent, who on May 30, 1996, had been convicted of two counts of distribution of cocaine in violation of 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1), ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1997); and ordered the respondent deported from the United States. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal from that decision by order dated December 6, 1999.
 
On September 11, 2006, when this matter was last before us, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our April 10, 2006, decision. In the April 10, 2006, decision, we denied his motion to reopen as time and number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In the instant motion, styled “Lozada Motion to Reopen,” the respondent argues that the proceedings should be reopened pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) to permit him to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c).1 The respondent contends that he would have earlier applied for a waiver of inadmissibility under the St. Cyr decision had it not been for the ineffective assistance of his previous counsel. In support of his motion, the respondent provided a copy of the formal complaint that he has filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority against his former counsel, a copy of a letter notifying his former counsel of the complaint and giving him the opportunity to respond to the charges, and affidavits setting out the basis of his claim against his former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Even if the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, he does not benefit from the St. Cyr. decision and cannot therefore demonstrate prejudice. See Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98 (3rd Cir. 2005); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. We find that the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim fails and the motion will be denied on that basis.
 
*2 In INS v. St. Cyr, supra, the Supreme Court held that discretionary relief under former § 212(c) “remains available for aliens … whose convictions were obtained through plea agreements and who … would have been eligible for § 212(c) relief at the time of their plea under the law then in effect.” See INS v. St. Cyr, supra, at 326. Here, the deportation proceedings were initiated against the respondent on March 27, 1997, with the filing of the Order to Show Cause (Form I-221) with the Immigration Court. The law in effect at the time of the respondent’s May 30, 1996, conviction was the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA), Pub. L. No. 104-132, 110 Stat. 1214), which was enacted on April 24, 1996. Thus, the respondent, who subsequent to April 24, 1996 was convicted of an aggravated felony, was not eligible for section 212(c) relief when his guilty plea was entered on May 30, 1996. See INS v. St. Cyr, supra; DiPeppe v. Quarantillo, 337 F.3d 326 (3rd Cir. 2003); Perez v. Elwood, 294 F.3d 552 (3d Cir. 2002); DeSousa v. Reno, 190 F.3d 175 (3d Cir. 1999). See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44 (section 212(c) available where proceedings initiated before April 24, 1996).
 
In addition, the record shows that in late July 2001 or early August 2001, the respondent was deported from the United States. See Respondent’s Affidavit in support of the instant motion. He may not reopen the original immigration proceedings to apply for a waiver of deportation because he left the United States after those proceedings were initiated. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)(2) (specifically providing that special motions to reopen are unavailable to aliens “issued a final order of deportation … who then illegally returned to the United States”). We note that the respondent did not state in his affidavit whether he has returned to the United States and if so, under what circumstances. In any event, the respondent’s departure deprived this Board of jurisdiction to reopen the proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

Our records show that although the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not respond to the respondent’s motion to reconsider, the instant brief was fast filed herein as “Respondent’s Response to Government’s Opposition” on September 11, 2006, the same date that our previous decision was issued. Given the respondent’s expression of dissatisfaction with his former counsel’s performance in his brief and supporting affidavit, we noted the respondent’s failing to satisfy the procedural requirements of and demonstrate prejudice as required by Matter of Lozada.

2006 WL 3922206 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CIPRIANO CORDOVA MENDOZA
FILE: A37 801 647 - SAN FRANCISCO
December 14, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank P. Sprouls, Esq.
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Continuance
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 7, 2006, finding the respondent removable pursuant to sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (2)(A)(i)(II), as having been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude as well as having been convicted of a controlled substance violation, based on his record of conviction1 (Exh. 2, 3) and ordering his removal to Mexico, will be dismissed.
 
We find no merit to the contentions raised by the respondent on appeal. Specifically, the respondent argues on appeal that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel by his criminal attorney in that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea as to his 1998 California controlled substance conviction (Exh. 2), and the Immigration Judge erred in not granting his request for a further continuance while awaiting action on his appeal of the denial of his post-conviction motion to set aside and vacate the conviction in that case (Tr. at 6-7).
 
At the outset, even if we were to accept the respondent’ s assertion that the attorney at his criminal trial did not explain the immigration consequences of a guilty plea, he has not demonstrated that his counsel’s actions were objectively unreasonable so as to constitute ineffective assistance of counsel and a violation of due process, undermining the validity of the respondent’s 1998 California drug trafficking conviction (Exh. 2) upon which one of the charges of removability is premised and precluding his eligibility for relief from removal. See United States v. Fry, 322 F.3d 1198, 1200 (9th Cir. 2003) (quoting the U.S. Supreme Court’s decision in Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 694 (1984) as to the standards required for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in a criminal proceeding). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, citing to its decision in United States v. Fry, supra at 1200, has held that “the failure of counsel to advise his client of the potential immigration consequences of a conviction does not violate the Sixth Amendment right to effective assistance of counsel.” See Resendiz v. Kovensky, 416 F.3d 952, 957 (9th Cir. 2005). The court in U.S. v. Kwan, 407 F.3d 1005, 1015 (9th Cir. 2005) observed that “¿wºhile ¿the Ninth Circuit hasº not yet determined what ‘more’ would cause a defense Counsel’s advice, or lack thereof, regarding the immigration consequences of a conviction to fall below an objective standard of reasonableness, ¿the court notedº that the Second Circuit ¿in United States v. Couto, 311 F.3d 179, 187-88 (2d Cir. 2002)ºhas held that an affirmative misrepresentation regarding immigration consequences is deficient under Strickland.” However, in the matter before us, the respondent does not allege any “affirmative misrepresentation regarding immigration consequences” by his criminal attorney, only that she failed to advise him as to the possible immigration consequences of his guilty plea.
 
*2 Notwithstanding, it is clear that an Immigration Judge and the Board cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction, unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to examine an alien’s claims regarding his guilt or innocence. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). In addition, the Ninth Circuit has held that the Immigration Judge and the Board may not adjudicate the validity of an underlying criminal conviction. See Pablo v. INS, 72 F.3d 110, 113 (9th Cir. 1995) (the court observed that “¿tºhe BIA correctly refused, in effect, to retry these criminal cases based on ¿alienº’s claim that he was innocent of the charges although he pleaded guilty to them”’; De la Cruz v. INS, 951 F.2d 226, 228 (9th Cir. 1991) (Upon petitioner’s claim that he was denied effective assistance of counsel in his state court criminal trial because he was not advised of the deportation consequences of pleading guilty, the court recited that the “INS has no power to adjudicate the validity of state convictions underlying deportation hearings”). We observe, moreover, that a conviction is deemed final when all direct appeals have been exhausted or waived. See Matter of Ozkok, 19 I&N Dec. 546 (BIA 1988). The pendency of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attacks, not constituting direct appeals, however, does not serve to negate the finality of the convictions or the charge of deportability, unless and until the conviction has been overturned pursuant to that motion. See Grageda v. INS, 12 F.3d 919, 921 (9th Cir. 1993).
 
In addition, the respondent is precluded from collaterally attacking his criminal record of conviction in a deportation proceeding. See Urbina-Mauricio v. INS, 989 F.2d 1085, 1089 (9th Cir. 1993) (citing Avila-Murrieta v. INS, 762 F.2d 733, 736 (9th Cir.1985) and Ocon-Perez v. INS, 550 F.2d 1153, 1154 (9th Cir. 1977)). Moreover, the court in Urbina-Mauricio found that “¿aºcriminal conviction is final for the purposes of immigration review if the alien has exhausted or waived direct appellate review.” Urbina-Mauricio, supra at 1089 (citing Hernandez-Almanza v. INS, 547 F.2d 100, 103 (9th Cir. 1976)). Furthermore, the fact that a criminal defendant is not advised of the collateral immigration consequences of his plea does not amount to a denial of due process that would vitiate the plea. See U.S. v. Amador-Leal, 276 F.3d 511, 517 (9th Cir. 2002); see also United States v. Osiemi, 980 F.2d 344, 349 (5th Cir. 1993).
 
*3 Moreover, the decision to grant or deny a continuance is in the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown. See De La Cruz v. INS, supra; see also Matter of Luviano-Rodriguez, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). That decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Luviano-Rodriguez, supra at 237; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6. There is no indication in the record that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing. The record clearly indicates that the conviction record (Exhs. 2, 3) entered into evidence relates to the respondent and is final and not under direct appeal, and supports the Immigration Judge’s findings as to the charges of removability (Tr. at 7-8).
 
An Immigration Judge presented with a facially valid charge of removability need not hold removal proceedings in abeyance based on the mere possibility that a collateral criminal proceeding could have a future impact on the alien’s immigration status. See Contreras v. Schiltgen, 122 F.3d 30, 33-34 (9th Cir. 1997), aff’d on add’l grounds in Contreras v. Schiltgen, 151 F.3d 906 (9th Cir. 1998) (Contreras II) (the court found that the “validity of ¿the alien’sº California conviction can only be tested in an action against the state, which has the greatest interest in preserving its judgment and the best ability to either correct or defend it …and … ¿uºntil ¿the alienº has successfully overturned his conviction in such a proceeding, the ¿Immigration Judgeº is entitled to rely on the conviction as a basis for custody and eventual deportation.”). The respondent had already received three continuances over a period of 5 months within which time he was unsuccessful in his collateral attack of his criminal conviction (Tr. at 6), and the Immigration Judge considered that it would be speculative that the respondent would be successful in the appeal of the denial of his petition for a writ of habeas corpus (Tr. at 6-7). See Baires v. INS, 856 F.2d 89, 91, 92-93 (9th Cir. 1988) (discussing factors the Immigration Judge must consider when adjudicating a request for a continuance).
 
In addition, we note that approximately 3 months have elapsed since the Immigration Judge rendered his decision in this matter, and we have yet to receive any indication from the respondent that he has prevailed in his collateral criminal proceedings. Consequently, as observed above, no purpose other than an unnecessary delay of the proceedings would be served by a further continuance, and the Immigration Judge exercised sound discretion in denying the request. See Matter of Sibrun, supra at 356-57. Therefore, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision not to allow the respondent a further continuance to collaterally challenge the conviction. Because the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion by denying the respondent’s request for a further continuance, his appellate claim of a due process violation fails. See Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir.2000) (to prevail on a procedural due process challenge to deportation proceedings, alien must show error and substantial prejudice).
 
*4 The respondent has presented no arguments on appeal that would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that he is not inadmissible under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) and (2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, and is subject to removal from the United States based on the conviction record submitted in this case (Exhs. 2, 3). See section 240(c)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(2)(A). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

The record reflects that on April 16, 1998, the respondent was convicted in the California Superior Court, County of Monterey, California, of the offense of Possession or Purchase for Sale of a Controlled Substance, a felony, in violation of section 11351 of the California Health and Safety Code (Exh. 2). Furthermore, the record reflects that on February 28, 2003, the respondent was convicted in the California Superior Court, County of Salinas, California, of the offense of Inflict Corporal Injury on a Spouse/Cohabitant, a misdemeanor, in violation of section 273.5(a) of the California Penal Code (Exh. 3). See Matter of Tran, 21 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1996) (willful infliction of corporal injury on a spouse, cohabitant, or parent of the perpetrator’s child, in violation of section 273.5(a) of the California Penal Code, constitutes a crime involving moral turpitude).

2006 WL 3712522 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHALID UMER
FILE: AXX XX2 967 - SAN ANTONIO
December 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Omar Cura, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anibal D. Martinez
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2005, order finding him removable, deeming his application for relief to be abandoned, and ordering him removed to Pakistan. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
At proceedings held on June 14, 2005, the respondent conceded removability as charged (Tr. at 15-16), and was directed by the Immigration Judge to file his application for relief in compliance with local procedures, noting that the court required strict adherence to those procedures. The Immigration Judge asked if there were any questions. Neither the respondent nor his counsel raised any questions concerning the court’s filing requirements. Nevertheless, the respondent’s application was not timely filed, and the Immigration Judge cancelled the merits hearing and entered an order of removal.
 
The respondent’s notice of appeal and brief list several explanations for the failure to timely file the application. One explanation was that there was bad advice from the clerk’s office as to when the application could be filed. This explanation was proffered in the form of a statement by counsel. Statements or assertions by counsel are not evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). Nothing else has been submitted to support the respondent’s claim in this regard.
 
Counsel for the respondent further claims that the filing was too complex to be successfully accomplished in a timely fashion. The record does not support this claim. The transcript of record makes it clear that the Immigration Judge provided an opportunity for questions about filing and no objection was made to the scheduling of the merits hearing (Tr. at 17-18). It is clear from the record that the respondent did not object to the filing deadline.
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge caused the filing to be late by arbitrarily rejecting the filed application for minor technical reasons, such as counsel’s failure to mark the box indicating he was not subject to disciplinary procedures. The assertion that any action of the Immigration Judge caused the application to be late is not supported by the record. The respondent also argues that he should not suffer from his counsel’s mistakes. However, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim has not been made. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting forth the procedure for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*2 In sum, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the application was abandoned. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (providing that an Immigration Judge may set reasonable time limits for the filing of applications and the failure to file within the time set means the opportunity to file is deemed waived); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549-50 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3712528 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BEATRICE ATZEL
FILE: AXX XX3 990 - MIAMI
December 7, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Neil F. Lewis, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge decision denying her request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). She has also requested that we remand the record due to ineffective assistance of her previous counsel, for the presentation of new and material evidence, and based on her argument that the Notice to Appear was improper. The appeal will be sustained in part and the record remanded for further proceedings.
 
The respondent has for the most part compiled with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id at 640. While it is not entirely clear from the record whether the respondent’s previous attorney acted ineffectively to such an extent as to prejudice the respondent, the respondent has now presented significant additional evidence, some of which could have been produced at the previous hearing, perhaps indicating ineffective assistance, and some of which was unavailable, requiring that the record be remanded for consideration of the new evidence. In particular, we note the possible lapse of judgement on the part of previous counsel in not calling the respondent’s United States citizen husband to testify on the respondent’s behalf although he was present at the time. It appears he will suffer meaningful hardship were the respondent to be removed from the United States, a factor only briefly considered by the Immigration Judge in his decision. We also note that although the respondent, through previous counsel, admitted to the allegations against her, the charging document does, in fact, indicate an incorrect statute under which she was convicted.
 
*2 Given the problems outlined above, we find we should remand the record for further consideration of the respondent’s removability and her application for cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings.
