KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Case Reopened by IN RE: ADONAY REMBERTO ORTIZ, BIA, April 21, 2011
2006 WL 3088919 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ORTIZ, ADONAY REMBERTO
FILE: AXX-XX7-988 - LOS ANGELES
September 19, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vargas, Patricia, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 11, 2006. The respondent’s case was last before us on January 4, 2002, when we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with an opportunity to seek a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, 121 S. Ct. 2271 (2001). The record reflects that the respondent was present with counsel on October 13, 2005, when the Immigration Judge provided notice concerning the next hearing to be held on January 19, 2006. On January 19, 2006, the respondent failed to appear. On March 28, 2006, he submitted a motion to reopen, and claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. He stated that he failed to appear due to the specific instruction from his counsel that he did not need to appear for that hearing because it would be continued. The respondent has explained his decision not to file a bar complaint. The issue on appeal is whether the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent’s counsel had appeared at the hearing and verified that the respondent had notice of the hearing. She concluded that the respondent had not demonstrated substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
According to the respondent’s affidavit, two different individuals from the law firm that continues to represent him told him he did not need to appear for the hearing on January 19, 2006. Both individuals named by the respondent, Mr. Duran and Mr. Ramos, have submitted entry of appearance forms indicating they were admitted to the bar in the state of California. Under these circumstances, and considering that Ms. Vargas confirmed on the day of the last hearing that the respondent had proper notice, we do not find this to be a situation where full compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is unnecessary. It is not clear from the record who is to blame for the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing for which he admittedly received proper notice from the Immigration Judge. If a properly licensed attorney told the respondent he need not appear under these circumstances, then compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is necessary to assign responsibility for this ineffective assistance. The respondent’s suggestion that he received “erroneous instructions” which “do not merit a complaint to the State Bar” is unpersuasive because it is not merely the respondent’s prerogative as to whether or not to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. Given the different attorneys who were apparently involved in the respondent’s representation, this is simply not a case where “the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The Immigration Judge appropriately denied reopening. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3088894 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MATEO SARABIA-BOJORQUEZ
FILE: A20 687 951 - EL PASO
A79 041 7811
September 18, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy W. Apostol, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stephen M. Ruhle
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated April 26, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the order of deportation that had been entered on May 14, 1984, in deportation proceedings which had been conducted in absentia under section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b), due to the respondent’s failure to appear. The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he had no knowledge that he had ever been placed under deportation proceedings. Moreover, he disclaims any knowledge of the attorney who represented him in those proceedings, and in any event, maintains that he did not receive notice of the deportation hearing scheduled for May 14, 1984, and that said attorney failed to notify him of that hearing and his actions constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, and establish reasonable cause for his failure to appear.
 
The respondent’s allegations are belied by the record. At the outset, we consider the respondent received personal service of the Order to Show Cause (form I-221S) on June 10, 1983, while he was detained at the El Paso Processing Center. The respondent’s fingerprint and signature were placed on the Order to Show Cause at the time it was served (Exh. 1). Furthermore, the record indicates the respondent retained the attorney Paul Douglass to represent him as evidenced by the Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney (Form G-28) filed on the respondent’s behalf and dated June 14, 1983 (Exh. 2). The respondent’s counsel, Paul Douglass, then filed a motion for bond redetermination dated June 14, 1983 (Exh. 3), and the respondent’s bond was reduced to $500 after a bond hearing held on June 14, 1983 (Exh. 4). On June 15, 1983, the respondent posted bond and was released from custody, and the respondent’s counsel, Paul Douglass, was the obligor for this bond (Exh. 5). Upon his release from custody, the respondent was served with a notice that his hearing had been rescheduled to an undetermined future date (Exh. 6). On June 30, 1983, the Immigration Court sent notice of the respondent’s rescheduled hearing for May 14, 1984, to his attorney Paul Douglass, by certified mail, return receipt requested (Exh. 7). This notice was accepted and signed for by the office of his attorney Paul Douglass on July 1, 1983 (Exh. 8). The record reflects the respondent failed to appear for the rescheduled hearing. See I.J. Dec. at 2. Since the respondent failed to appear for the hearing on May 14, 1984, he was ordered deported in absentia to Mexico (Exh. 9).
 
*2 We find that the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the Immigration Court provided proper notice of the hearing to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Moreover, we find no support for the respondent’s contention that his attorney of record failed to inform him that he had a scheduled hearing which resulted in the order of deportation being entered in his absence.
 
We have held that to satisfy a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must support his claim by a detailed affidavit, inform his previous attorney of the allegations of misconduct and provide the attorney with an opportunity to respond, and state whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the attorney’s alleged violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the prongs described in Matter of Lozada, supra. Accordingly, we find that the respondent has not satisfied a Lozada showing of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997) (burden of proving ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Consequently, we find that the respondent received proper notice of his hearing and that he has not provided any reasonable cause of his failure to appear. See Matter of Ruiz, 20 I&N Dec. 91 (BIA 1989); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988); Matter of Nafi, 19 I&N Dec. 430 (BIA 1987). The respondent has presented no arguments on appeal that would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

Both parties shall likewise modify their records accordingly and will ensure that the new alien registration number is used in any future correspondence involving this respondent.

2006 WL 3088898 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUBEN VERGARA-PALOMINO
FILE: A73 837 556 - LOS ANGELES
September 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan R. Diamante, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
This matter was last before the Board on May 11, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen filed December 14, 2005, as being time and numbers barred and finding that equitable tolling of the requirements otherwise governing reopening requests was not warranted in this case.1 On June 9, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider our May 11, 2006, decision. The motion is denied.
 
Pursuant to the federal regulations, a motion to reconsider should specify the claimed errors of fact or law in the Board’s prior order and should be supported by pertinent authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991), aff’d, 979 F.2d 212 (11th Cir. 1992) (table case). The respondent asserts that in denying his motion to reopen, the Board did not take into consideration the full breadth of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim as it relates to the conduct of Ms. Laura Friend, or his claims that he was prejudiced thereby in various ways. See Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider (“Motion to Reconsider”).
 
More particularly, the respondent explains that his ineffective assistance claim is based on factors in addition to the bad advice Ms. Friend gave that resulted in his being placed in removal proceedings at time when he was ineligible for relief. For example, he points out that Ms. Friend misrepresented to him that she was a licensed attorney. However, in our decision we expressly noted his claim that Ms. Friend, a non-attorney, held herself out to be an attorney. See Board order (“Order”), May 11, 2006, p. 1. The respondent also contends that Ms. Friend submitted a Form G-28 in Immigration Court claiming to be licensed to practice in Wisconsin. However, this form was not filed in Immigration Court in the respondent’s case, and it is the respondent’s case that is the only one before us now for consideration. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen as amended, p. 55. In any event, we reiterate that in our prior order we explicitly noted the respondent’s claim that Ms. Friend held herself out to be an attorney. See Order, p. 1.
 
The respondent also contends that Ms. Friend provided ineffective assistance to him by continually advising him that his case was being effectively handled, and by her illegal practice of law as evidenced by her “having attorneys submit an appeal with the Board and a motion to reopen on ¿the respondent’sº behalf both of which were filed untimely, under her supervision.” See Motion to Reconsider, p. 8. However, contrary to the respondent’s assertion, his appeal was timely filed. It is true that his subsequent motion was untimely filed and was denied on this basis, but aside from this consideration that motion was not grantable because a prima facie showing of eligibility for section 245(i) adjustment of status had not been made, inasmuch as the underlying labor certification had not been approved and the employment-based visa petition based on an approved labor certification had not been filed, much less approved.
 
*2 Regarding his claim of the “ill-handling” of his labor certification petition, our prior order did not explicitly discuss this specific contention. However, we did address his assertion that Ms. Friend was to blame for his inability to affirmatively apply for adjustment of status. See Order, p. 2. His eligibility for such relief, of course, rested on actions taken with respect to the underlying labor certification and employment-based visa petition.
 
The respondent also asserts that we did not consider his claim that the delay in the approval of the pertinent labor certification was the direct result of Ms. Friend’s ineffective assistance and incompetence. See Motion to Reconsider, p. 9. Even assuming that this claim was not fully addressed in our May 11, 2006, decision, the claim itself is speculative in nature. The record reflects that the labor certification application in question was prepared and filed soon after the respondent met with Ms. Friend. See attachments to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, filed November 16, 1999 (“Motion to Reopen”). It was assigned a priority date of March 31, 1997, and although it took a lengthy period of time for it to be reviewed and approved, it has not been established by the respondent’s motion to reopen or his amended motion to reopen that this delay was attributable directly or primarily to actions or inactions on the part of Ms. Friend, or that Ms. Friend’s handling of the labor certification application (which was ultimately approved) or the strategic decisions made regarding this aspect of his case amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel.2 Although he asserts, through counsel, that the labor certification process “could” have been completed faster, there was not an adequate showing that this would have been the case, particularly given the many issues and steps involved in securing government approval of a labor certification application and/or a corresponding employment-based visa petition. See Motion to Reopen, pp. 1-2.
 
The respondent contends that “his labor certification could have been completed faster, since by placing him and his wife in removal proceedings Ms. Friend caused jurisdiction to vest in the immigration court and the immigration service no longer had jurisdiction to approve his adjustment of status application. This in of itself delayed the processing of ¿the respondent’sº labor certification petition from being approved until June 17, 2002, and this delayed processing was clearly not a natural processing delay.” See Motion to Reconsider, p. 10. The record does not support this assertion. To the contrary, the record reflects that despite the facts that the respondent was placed in removal proceedings, ordered removed by an Immigration Judge on May 1, 1998, and his appeal of that order was dismissed by this Board on June 18, 1999, the processing of the labor certification continued, the labor certification was ultimately approved, the corresponding employment-based visa petition was filed on March 21, 2001, and that it was approved on June 17, 2002. See Amended Motion to Reopen, Tabs D and E, pp. 12, 13. It is has not been established that his proceedings in Immigration Court adversely affected the independent processing of the labor certification or the related visa petition. He became eligible for adjustment of status only after these actions occurred and years after his removal proceedings were completed. See Order, p. 2. Indeed, his adjustment application was not filed with the former Immigration and Naturalization Service until June 28, 2002. See Amended Motion to Reopen, Tab D. Thus, the jurisdictional argument advanced by the respondent is unavailing.
 
*3 In his motion to reconsider, the respondent also asserts that the holding in Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968 (9th Cir. 2004), which we relied on in our prior decision, is inapplicable here. See Motion to Reconsider, pp. 11-12. We disagree and stand by our prior decision in this regard.
 
With respect to the respondent’s contention that he was denied a full and fair hearing due to Ms. Friend’s ineffective assistance, this contention also is without merit. See Motion to Reconsider, pp. 15-16, 20. Ms. Friend did not represent the respondent in Immigration Court, attorney Linda Howard did, and there has been no showing that her legal representation of him in those proceedings was incompetent or would support an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against her (Tr. at 1-10). Indeed, the respondent has elected not to proceed on such a claim and has not filed a bar complaint against attorney Howard. See Motion to Reconsider, pp. 6, 21-22. Although he now contends that he was denied the opportunity to have counsel of his choice represent him in his proceedings before the Immigration Judge, it was the respondent himself who confirmed to the Immigration Judge in two separate hearings that Ms. Howard was his attorney. See Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider, p. 20; Tr. at 1 (January 27, 1998, hearing); Tr. at 5 (May 1, 1998, hearing).3 He registered no objection to Ms. Howard representing him.
 
With respect to his claim that the motion filed by attorney Robert F. Jacobs wasted his “one opportunity to reopen his case and have his adjustment of status through his labor certification case adjudicated,” we are not persuaded. See Motion to Reconsider, p. 21. The motion filed by attorney Jacobs was in fact premised on a claim of eligibility for adjustment of status based on the labor certification that was pending and given that visas were then available in the relevant preference category. However, as discussed above, although that motion was denied as being untimely filed, it could not have been granted because his prima facie eligibility for section 245(i) adjustment of status was not demonstrated. Moreover, his argument is conjectural that “but for” Ms. Friend’s handling of the labor certification/employment-based visa petition these applications would have been approved prior to the filing of his first motion or at some point prior thereto. In any event, the respondent has elected not to pursue an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Jacobs. See Motion to Reconsider, p. 6, 22.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.

2006 WL 3088897 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DE YOU ZHENG
FILE: AXX XX2 820 - NEW YORK CITY
September 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew Guadagno, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º-Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent moves to reopen our decision dated April 19, 2002, dismissing his appeal. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of prior counsel by Ren Rong Pan, Esquire, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal before the Board. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision. The motion would have been due on July 18, 2002. While motions to reopen premised on ineffective assistance may be subject to an equitable toll, the respondent has not established that he exercised the due diligence required for such tolling. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir.2006) (citing Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 128 (2d Cir.2000)). The court stated in Cekic, “Tolling is available if the alien can demonstrate that (1) his counsel’s conduct violated ¿hisº constitutional right to due process, and (2) the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period ¿heº seeks to toll.” Id. To make this showing, an alien must demonstrate that he pursued his case “¿fºrom the point at which the ineffective assistance of counsel should have been, or was in fact, discovered.” See id. at 171 (citing Iavorski, 232 F.3d at 135). It is the respondent’s burden to establish that the 90-day deadline should be equitably tolled, but his motion is lacking in detail, and he has thus failed to establish due diligence. See Cekic at 170 noting that “no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.” It appears, moreover, that the respondent should have discovered the alleged ineffective assistance of prior counsel at least by 2003, since the Board had affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision and dismissed the appeal in 2002. The respondent did not file the instant motion until June 27, 2006, years after his direct appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision had been dismissed, and years after he had retained Mr. Pan. In the affidavit the respondent admits he was dissatisfied with prior counsel’s representation when his appeal was dismissed by the Board, but he took no prompt action. The respondent cannot be considered to have diligently pursued his case during the period he now seeks to toll. See also Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517-18 (2d Cir.2006) (concluding that the Board did not abuse its discretion in finding that alien had not exercised reasonable diligence). Accordingly, the respondent does not qualify for equitable tolling.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 3088895 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO ST. CLOUD-BONEQUI A.K.A. FRANK ST. CLOUD
FILE: A13 086 511 - SAN FRANCISCO
September 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emil J. Molin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace H. Cheung
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on May 31, 2005, when we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge in order to obtain a complete transcript and record of proceedings. The Immigration Judge indicates that, upon review of the tapes and transcript, the record is in fact complete. Thus, we will now consider the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 2004, decision and order, finding the respondent to be removable as charged and finding that all applications for relief were waived for failure to timely submit any applications. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). The respondent requests a remand for further proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
The respondent, in his Notice of Appeal, indicates that the Immigration Judge’s decision constituted an abuse of discretion and that it was contrary to law. However, these averments were never supported or explained. Rather, in his appeal brief, the respondent simply submitted a request to remand the record for further proceedings, indicating that the respondent was eligible for 212(c) relief and indicating that the respondent was still trying to prove that his father was a United States citizen. As the respondent has not given any reasons supporting his allegations of error and abuse of discretion1, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that there is any reversible error in the instant case.
 
Concerning 212(c), the respondent has not given any explanation why he failed to ever submit an application for 212(c) relief. At the August 1, 2001, status conference, the respondent’s counsel was given 9 months to prepare and submit a form I-191 (a 212(c) application). See August 1, 2001, transcript, at p.4. The respondent’s counsel was told three times that he must submit the 212(c) application by May 1, 2002. Id., at p.4-6. However, no such application was ever submitted, nor was there any request for an extension to file such application. See February 2, 2004, transcript, at p. 15. Hence, the Immigration Judge properly indicated that such application was deemed waived. Id., at p. 16; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (an Immigration Judge has the authority to set a time limit for the filing of applications for relief, and any applications not filed within that time limit shall be deemed waived).
 
*2 The respondent has requested a remand, in order to have the opportunity to file a 212(c) application. However, no explanation has ever been given for failing to meet the previous filing deadline; thus, we have found that such application was properly deemed waived. Furthermore, even if such application was not waived, such application was required to be submitted for consideration of a remand for the purpose of pursuing such application. A motion to remand is subject to the same requirements as a motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(1), (c)(4); see also Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999) (indicating that a motion to reopen filed during the pendency of an appeal may be styled as a motion to remand; however, in substance it remains a motion to reopen); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992). In order to obtain a remand of the record, for the purpose of submitting an application for relief, such motion must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation. Id. Here, the respondent failed to attach an application for 212(c) relief, in addition to supporting documentation, to his appeal or motion. Thus, even if the 212(c) application had not previously been waived, the respondent’s motion to remand would be denied for failure to submit the application with his motion.
 
The respondent also claims that he is still attempting to prove that his father was a United States citizen. We note that the respondent indicated that there were documents that were damaged or destroyed and he was pursuing other means of documenting his father’s United States citizen status, as of the August 1, 2001, status conference. See August 1, 2001, transcript, at p.2; see also February 2, 2004, transcript, at p. 16. It has now been more than 5 years since the respondent indicated that there were damaged military documents and that he was continuing to pursue other sources of documentation. There is no evidence that any other documentation has ever been discovered or presented. Neither the respondent, nor the respondent’s counsel, have given any indication concerning what steps have been taken to further pursue any documentation. We can not indefinitely postpone a hearing while the respondent continues to search for documentation. The respondent’s counsel, at the August 1, 2001, status conference, was told to pursue this documentation and to file a form N-600. See August 1, 2001, transcript, at p.2. There is no indication that this has been diligently pursued or that such form has ever been filed. We can not find that the respondent has established that there is sufficient evidence that he is a United States citizen or that he has derived such status. Thus, we can not find any error in this regard. We note that there is no indication that a form N-600 was ever filed and such form was not presented with the motion to remand. We do not find that there has been sufficient reason given to remand the record, concerning the respondent’s claim to United States citizenship, in the instant case.
 
*3 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion to remand is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

Although the respondent never specifically mentions his prior oral motion for a continuance, made at his February 2, 2004, hearing, we will address such motion in dicta in this footnote. We note that an oral motion for a continuance was made at the February 2, 2004, hearing by the respondent, in the absence of his counsel. The respondent stated that his counsel was ill, and that the counsel was trying to vacate one of his criminal convictions. The Immigration Judge noted that such counsel never submitted anything in writing requesting a continuance. We note that there is no affidavit from the respondent’s counsel, regarding the alleged illness. A motion to reopen was not filed. There is no doctor’s note or other evidence in the record supporting the respondent’s averments concerning his counsel’s health. The Immigration Judge indicated that the respondent had received a total of seven continuances during proceedings, four of which occurred after the venue of the proceedings was changed. Thus, although this is dicta, we note that the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by denying the respondent’s oral motion for a continuance at the February 2, 2004, hearing. We further note that the respondent has not alleged that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, there is no reversible error established and no adequate reason to remand the record.

2006 WL 3203596 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERETT EARL JACKSON
FILE: AXX XX7 540 - HOUSTON
September 15, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º-Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on June 2, 2005, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s May 7, 2004, decision finding the respondent removable as charged, statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal, and denying his application for voluntary departure. The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, on August 22, 2006, filed a motion to reopen. He acknowledges that the motion was not filed in a timely fashion but urges that the proceedings should be reopened by reason of his submission of evidence showing prima facie eligibility for relief as well as evidence which is material. He also requests reopening by reason of ineffective assistance of counsel, both during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge, and on appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date that decision was rendered, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the instant case, the Board issued a decision on June 2, 2005. A motion to reopen would therefore have been due on or before Thursday, September 1, 2005. As the respondent acknowledges, the motion was untimely where it was filed on August 22, 2006, approximately 12 months after it was due. The respondent claims that the filing deadline should be ignored as he is eligible for relief and has submitted material evidence. However, those are the requirements which must be satisfied for the Board to grant a timely filed motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
In our precedent decision in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), we emphasized the importance of the time limits on motions to reopen, noting the need to discourage dilatory motions and Congress’ mandate that we do so. We further recognized our limited discretionary power to reopen, as well as the need to exercise such power only in exceptional situations. Id., see also Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). The burden is on the respondent to demonstrate that an exceptional situation exists. The respondent in this case has failed to meet that burden. The respondent identifies June 2, 2005, the date of the Board decision, as the last date of his claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. In that decision, we found that he had failed to meet the procedural requirements as set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), and that he had failed to show prejudice. In his motion he fails to address why his untimely motion to reopen should be considered, given that nothing in his motion presents a circumstance which precluded him from timely filing his motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed out of time, and we find that exercising our discretion to reopen this matter is not in order.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent intended his “Motion to Reopen and Reconsider” to be considered a motion to reconsider, we point out that it is untimely. A motion to reconsider must be filed within 30 days after the mailing of the Board decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The Board’s decision in this case was entered on June 2, 2005. Thus, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before July 5, 2005. Accordingly, to the extent that we consider the respondent’s motion as a motion to reconsider, it must be considered to have been filed out of time.
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GONZALEZ DE LA LUNA MA. C.
FILE: AXX XX0 215 - CHICAGO
September 14, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maribel Herrera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sylke Merchan,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on November 28, 2001. On March 21, 2006, she filed a motion to reopen with an Immigration Judge requesting an opportunity to be considered for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on April 13, 2006, and this appeal followed.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen to seek adjustment of status is untimely because it was filed more than 90 days after the order of removal was entered by the Immigration Judge. Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). On appeal, the respondent argues that an “exceptional circumstance” caused her to miss her hearing before the Immigration Judge. According to the respondent, her prior legal representatives were negligent, and these proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel. This issue was not raised before the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent’s claim for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was submitted more than 180 days after the removal order was entered, and is therefore untimely. We further find it inappropriate to equitably toll the statute of limitations for seeking reopening. See Pervaiz v. Gonzales, 405 F.3d 488 (7th Cir. 2005)(considering deadline for filing motion to reopen in the context of an in absentia hearing). The “test” for equitable tolling, as stated by the court in Pervaiz, supra, at 490, “… is whether the claimant could reasonably have been expected to file earlier.” The respondent has not explained why she waited approximately 5 years to seek reopening. In addition, the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for seeking reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7th Cir. 2001). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3088913 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARLON BARRIOS-HERNANDEZ
FILE: AXX XX1 504 - EL CENTRO
September 12, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Roberto G. Culas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeff Lindblad
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler.
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s June 1, 2006, decision ordering him removed from the United States as an alien who has knowingly assisted another alien to enter the United States in violation of law. See section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i) (2000). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The sole issue raised on appeal is whether the attorney who represented the respondent during his proceedings before the Immigration Judge provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to file an asylum application on the respondent’s behalf and by failing to investigate whether the respondent was in fact removable as charged. We dismiss the respondent’s appeal for several reasons. As a threshold matter, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor does the record show such a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance” as to warrant consideration of his claim despite his failure to comply with those requirements. Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 587-88 (9th Cir. 2006) (quoting Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000)). In this connection, we note that the respondent— while sworn—repeatedly admitted to the Immigration Judge that he had knowingly assisted another alien to enter the United States in violation of law, thereby supporting the charge of removability (Tr. at 15-16, 39-40). Moreover, although he initially stated that he had a fear of persecution or torture in El Salvador (Tr. at 17), he specifically disavowed that assertion—again, while sworn—in two subsequent hearings. (Tr. at 42, 45). Absent truly compelling circumstances not present here, an attorney does not provide ineffective assistance by declining to pursue lines of inquiry or forms of relief that have been expressly foreclosed by the sworn testimony of the client.
 
Finally, the respondent has not provided persuasive evidence of prejudice arising from counsel’s allegedly deficient performance, i.e, evidence reflecting a reasonable possibility that the outcome of his removal hearing would have been different had counsel provided more proficient representation. Ray v. Gonzales, supra, at 587; Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 824-26 (9th Cir. 2003). Specifically, the respondent has offered no evidence, either before the Immigration Judge or in connection with his appeal to this Board, to suggest that the charge of removability is unfounded or that he is in fact eligible for asylum despite his representations that he has no fear of persecution or torture in El Salvador. Although the respondent’s appellate brief contains a statement of counsel indicating that he conceded removability in order to protect his estranged spouse, who he claims was the real “culprit” in his alien smuggling activity, such statements by counsel are not evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
*2 Because the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is deficient on its face, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 3088826 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARVIN B. PAREDES
FILE: AXX XX3 377 - LOS ANGELES
September 11, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary D. Olive, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i) (I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s June 3, 2005, decision ordering him removed from the United States to his native Guatemala as an alien convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. See section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). On appeal, the respondent alleges that he was deprived of his right to a full and fair removal hearing because of ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he contends that his prior attorney was ineffective because he failed to arrange for the filing of an immigrant visa petition (Form I-130) on the respondent’s behalf, which could have formed the basis for a subsequent application for adjustment of status in Immigration Court had it been approved, and because he failed to inform the Immigration Judge that petitions for writs of coram nobis had been filed on his behalf in state court for the purpose of securing a vacatur of the convictions underlying the charge of removability.
 
As with all claims in which an alien argues that his “due process rights” were violated in removal proceedings, an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must show that he was prejudiced by the allegedly defective representation. Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005). Assuming arguendo that prior counsel’s failure to file the aforementioned documents on the respondent’s behalf constituted ineffective assistance, we conclude that the respondent has failed to demonstrate the requisite prejudice. Specifically, in the more than 14 months since the Immigration Judge rendered her decision in this matter, we have received no indication from the respondent that his criminal convictions have been vacated or modified in any way or that he has become the beneficiary of a visa petition that would form the basis for an adjustment of status application if approved.1 Accordingly, the respondent has not shown that prior counsel’s allegedly deficient performance had any effect at all on the outcome of his proceedings. In the absence of evidence indicating that there is any present basis for the respondent to challenge the validity of the charge of removability or to demonstrate that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status, we see no constructive purpose in remanding this matter for further proceedings.
 
The appeal is dismissed.

Moreover, we note that the Immigration Judge, in her decision, made it clear that continuances were granted for both purposes but respondent took no action (I.J. at 2).

2006 WL 3088963 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CRISTIAN ANDERS NIETO-ZUBIETA
FILE: AXX XX5 760 - SALT LAKE CITY
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rev. Michael R. Sciumbato, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mindy E. Hoeppner
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 5, 2006, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Subsequent to filing a notice of appeal, the respondent filed a motion to rescind and reopen with the Immigration Court on June 28, 2006. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on January 31, 2006. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear due to the disconnection of his cellular telephone, which resulted in the inability of the respondent’s former counsel to contact him regarding the new hearing date; and due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. Specifically, the respondent contends that his former counsel failed to inform him in writing of his next scheduled hearing, despite the fact that his former counsel was aware of the respondent’s current address. The respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish exceptional circumstances based on his claim that his cellular telephone was disconnected, and therefore, his former attorney was unable to contact him on his cellular telephone to inform him of the new hearing date. The respondent failed to provide any evidence regarding the dates of the alleged disconnection or the reason for the disconnection. Moreover, the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances due to disconnection of a cellular telephone does not qualify as an “exceptional circumstance” as defined section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
On appeal the respondent is represented by new counsel and now alleges ineffective assistance of counsel as the basis for his failure to appear. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) are satisfied. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal, including his affidavit, a letter to his former attorney informing him of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a complaint letter to the Utah State Bar Disciplinary Committee. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). However, we find such evidence probative of the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances due to ineffective assistance of counsel and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of this issue only. Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3088963 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3088952 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VASQUEZ-CHAVIRA, JAVIER
FILE: A78-895-543 - DENVER
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Duane Montano, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bonnie K. Brady,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen the proceeding to apply for adjustment of status based on his status as the spouse of a deceased United States citizen as provided in section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1151(b)(2).1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
An Immigration Judge has limited authority to hear an application for adjustment of status in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1). Relevant to the instant matter, the regulations provide that although there is no direct appeal from the decision of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to deny an adjustment application, the “applicant, if not an arriving alien, retains the right to renew his or her application” in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(5).
 
The record reflects that by notice (Form I- 191) dated December 5, 2002, the district direct of the Immigration and Naturalization Service (now the DHS) denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status.2 He is not now, however, seeking to renew that application; rather, the respondent has ignored the renewal procedure authorized in the regulations and is seeking reopening to apply for adjustment of status based on an entirely different visa petition. See Matter of Lasike, 17 I&N Dec. 445 (BIA 1980) (holding that an application may be considered “renewed” only when based on the same facts and reviewed according to circumstances as they existed when the original application was filed). We find no legal authority in the Act or in the regulations, and the respondent has not provided any legal authority, that permits him to seek adjustment of status based on a self-petition where, as here, the alien is in removal proceedings and a previously filed adjustment application has been denied by the DHS.
 
In any event, the respondent failed to support his motion to reopen with a completed application for adjustment of status (Form I-485) with supporting evidentiary material. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent has not shown that his self-petition has been approved or that he is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition filed by his now deceased United States citizen wife. See section 245 of the Act; Matter of Perez Vargas, 23 I&N Dec. 829 (BIA 2005) (The DHS has sole authority to adjudicate a visa petition.). Further, he has not provided any evidentiary material to show the likelihood that he will succeed in demonstrating the statutory requirements for a self-petition under section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Act. Consequently, the respondent has failed to provide prima facie proof of eligibility for adjustment of status under the Act. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); Matter of Sipus, 14 I&N Dec. 229 (BIA 1972).
 
*2 The respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge violated his right to due process by failing to inform him of the relief available to him during the removal hearing is foreclosed by United States v. Aguirre-Tello, 353 F.3d 1199 (10th Cir. 2004). In that decision, the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit, the circuit in which this cases arises, decided that a deportable alien does not have a constitutional right to be informed of discretionary relief that might be available to him or her. See id., at 1204. To the extent that the respondent is asserting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former counsel for “inexcusable neglect,” the claim fails because the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2

According to the Form I-191, the respondent failed to provide information concerning his convictions on February 28, 2001, and on March 18, 2002, for domestic abuse of his now deceased United States citizen wife. The record contains a copy of the February 12, 2004, judgment of the Municipal Court, City of Englewood, Arapaphoe Country, Colorado, in which the court vacated the February 28, 2001, conviction.

2006 WL 3088950 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS VELAZQUEZ-GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX4 575 - PORTLAND
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aaron Clingerman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas L. Day
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on June 10, 2005, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2004, decision denying the respondent’s application for adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4).
 
On March 29, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status. He also asks for a stay of voluntary departure.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s motion. It asserts that the respondent’s motion is not supported by material, previously unavailable evidence that supports reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing). The DHS also asserts that the respondent’s underlying approved immediate relative visa petition was automatically revoked when the petitioner, the respondent’s United States citizen spouse, died. See 8 C.F.R. § 205.1 (a)(3)(i)(C). Consequently, it asserts that the respondent is no longer statutorily eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i).
 
Initially, we observe that the respondent’s March 29, 2006, motion to reopen is time barred under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings, whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge, and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened). A final administrative order was entered in this case on June 10, 2005.
 
The respondent argues that the motions deadline should be equitably tolled based on his claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, Mr. James White. The respondent asserts that Mr. White failed to inform him of the status of his appeal and of his right to file a motion to reopen. The respondent has provided a declaration in which he claims that he first learned that this Board dismissed his appeal on February 10, 2006, during an interview with DHS officers. See Respondent’s Motion at 2. The respondent also correctly asserts that Mr. White was suspended from the practice of law in Oregon effective December 10, 2005, and was suspended from practice before this Board and the Immigration Courts effective January 11, 2006. See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals dated January 11, 2006. The respondent states that complete compliance with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), is unnecessary where, as here, the ineffectiveness is clear from the record.
 
*2 Equitable tolling of the time limits for motions to reopen is available to aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). We find that equitable tolling is appropriate in this case where the respondent substantially complied with the Lozada requirements, established that his case was prejudiced as a result of Mr. White’s misconduct, and, diligently filed this motion to reopen within a month of learning that his appeal was dismissed.
 
We now turn to the merits of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status. The DHS asserts that the respondent is no longer eligible for adjustment because his underlying visa petition was automatically revoked on the death of his United States citizen spouse.
 
However, pursuant to section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1151(b)(2)(A)(i), if the alien was the spouse of a United States citizen for two years before the citizen’s death and was not legally separated from the citizen at the time of the citizen’s death, the alien shall be considered to remain an immediate relative after the date of the citizen’s death but only if the spouse files a petition under 204(a)(1)(A)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(a)(1)(A)(ii), with in two years after such date and only until the date the spouse remarries. Although it is unclear when the respondent’s wife died, it appears that the respondent may be eligible for file a visa petition under this provision.
 
The respondent was granted a period of voluntary departure by this Board pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act. Such period of voluntary departure ended on August 9, 2005. The respondent failed to depart the United States during such time period. In most cases, an alien who fails to depart the United States within the time period specified in a voluntary departure order is, by virtue of that failure, rendered ineligible for adjustment of status for a period of 10 years. See section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). In this case, however, respondent’s failure to voluntarily depart or file a timely motion within the voluntary departure period was due to the clear ineffectiveness assistance of counsel. Accordingly, we find that the respondent is not barred from relief by operation of section 240B(d) of the Act. See generally Azarte v. Ashcroft, 394 F.3d 1278 (9th Cir. 2005) (holding “that in cases in which a motion to reopen is filed within the voluntary departure period and a stay of removal or voluntary departure is requested, the voluntary departure period is tolled during the period the ¿Boardº is considering the motion”).
 
*3 Based on the foregoing we find it appropriate to grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent can apply for adjustment of status and any other form of relief from removal for which he can establish eligibility. The appropriate orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 3203696 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE DANIEL BEJARAN A.K.A. JOSE DANIEL BEJARAN CHAVEZ
FILE: A90 119 233 - LAS VEGAS
August 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeremiah Wolf-Stuchiner, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; remand
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board of Immigration Appeals on May 26, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent now moves for reconsideration of our decision, and also requests that the record be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that he may apply for relief from removal. The motion will be denied.
 
On July 6, 2004, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States after denying his application for a waiver of deportability under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1994). The respondent did not file an appeal from that decision, and therefore it became a final administrative order of removal in August of 2004, upon expiration of the 30-day deadline for filing appeals with this Board. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39 (2004). In October of 2004 the respondent filed a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, alleging that his failure to file an appeal was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and requesting that the Immigration Judge reopen the proceedings and reissue his decision. On October 22, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, and the respondent appealed. In our decision of May 26, 2006, we concluded that the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen did not constitute reversible error because the respondent had not suffered prejudice as a result of his former attorney’s failure to appeal. Specifically, we concluded that the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver was not erroneous, particularly in light of the serious adverse discretionary factors present in his case. The respondent seeks reconsideration.
 
A motion to reconsider before this Board must specify the errors of fact or law contained in the previous order and must be supported by pertinent authority. See section 240(c)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(5)(C) (2000); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (2006). In his motion, the respondent simply asserts that he is statutorily eligible for section 212(c) relief, an issue that we questioned in a footnote in our May 26, 2006, decision. See Matter of Blake, 23 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 2005). Yet the respondent does not seriously dispute the central point of our decision, i.e., that the Immigration Judge correctly determined that he did not merit section 212(c) relief in the exercise of discretion, thereby precluding him from establishing prejudice in connection with his failure to appeal. We continue to espouse that position, and find that our decision of May 26, 2006, contained no material legal or factual error that would warrant reconsideration. We now turn to the respondent’s motion to remand.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent’s seeks a remand to apply for relief from removal, his motion will be denied because it is both untimely and number-barred. This Board’s motions regulation provides in pertinent part that “an alien may file only one motion to reopen removal proceedings (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). As we noted previously, the final administrative decision in this case was the Immigration Judge’s July 6, 2004, decision ordering the respondent removed and denying his request for a section 212(c) waiver. The respondent already filed one motion to reopen that decision in October of 2004 with the Immigration Judge, and therefore his present motion to remand is number-barred. Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (BIA 2000) (motions to remand are simply motions to reopen filed after appeal has been perfected, and they are subject to the same temporal and numerical limitations as other motions to reopen). Furthermore, the present motion is untimely because it was filed in June of 2006, significantly more than 90 days after the final administrative decision was rendered.1
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The 90-day regulatory filing deadline is not measured from this Board’s May 26, 2006, decision, which merely affirmed the Immigration Judge’s refusal to disturb the finality of the July 2004 removal order. The respondent does not obtain a new 90-day motion deadline by filing a meritless appeal from the denial of a prior motion. Cf. Matter of Oparah, supra.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIAN SALAZAR-ANAYA
FILE: A34 293 894 - LANCASTER
August 29, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard David Rogen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Barbara A. Hale
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent has filed an appeal from a May 1, 2006, decision of an Immigration Judge which found him removable as charged, ineligible for cancellation of removal for nonpermanent residents, and ordered him removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has replied that the decision of the Immigration Judge should be affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was ordered deported from the United States in 1989, as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation, after a hearing held in absentia in Los Angeles. The respondent did not appeal this order, and he was deported in 1990. The respondent subsequently reentered the United States, and was placed in these removal proceedings pursuant to a Notice to Appear dated July 11, 2005. During the course of the removal proceedings, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his deportation proceedings in Los Angeles based on ineffective assistance of counsel.1 The removal proceedings were continued on at least one occasion to see how the motion fared in the other court (see Tr. at 39). By the time of the final removal hearing, the motion to reopen deportation proceedings had been denied by the Immigration Judge in Los Angeles for a lack of jurisdiction.
 
The Immigration Judge in the instant case found the respondent removable as charged upon his admission that he was born abroad, and his failure to provide any evidence about his last entry into the United States. He also found the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal for nonpermanent residents due to his several convictions for controlled substance violations, and ordered him removed to Mexico.
 
The only issue raised on appeal is the respondent’s contention that his removal proceedings should have been continued by the Immigration Judge until his motion to reopen his deportation proceedings had been fully adjudicated. He points out that the Board dismissed his appeal of the denied motion on June 16, 2006, and that he now has an appeal pending with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit.
 
On review, we find that the Immigration Judge acted within his discretion in not continuing the removal proceedings pending further adjudication of the respondent’s motion to reopen. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29 (2006). The respondent’s motion to reopen arose in separate proceedings, and addressed a deportation order which was executed over 15 years earlier. Further, the Immigration Judge did in fact delay the proceedings to see what happened with the motion, and it had been denied at the time of the final removal hearing. The respondent is a detained alien, and the Immigration Judge acted appropriately in not indefinitely delaying the removal proceedings.2 Similarly, we reject the respondent’s argument that this Board should postpone its decision in these removal proceedings pending resolution of the appeal filed with the Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The respondent’s appeal will accordingly be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent argued that he was prejudiced because he would have pursued a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).

2

We also consider the Immigration Judge’s finding that regardless of what occurred with the motion to reopen the deportation proceedings, the respondent still had no proof that he was inspected and admitted at the time of his last entry into the United States.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Motion to Reopen Granted by IN RE: GUSTAVO ARROYO-MORALES, BIA, January 22, 2007
2006 WL 3203680 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO ARROYO-MORALES
FILE: A44 775 220 - CHICAGO
August 25, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claudia Valenzuela, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael Harper
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Dismissal of Aggravated Felony Charge
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 22, 2006, decision that ordered him removed from the United States as an alien convicted of aggravated felonies, controlled substance Violations, and two or more crimes involving moral turpitude. See sections 101(a)(43)(B), 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (a)(43)(B), 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), and 1227(a)(2)(B)(i). During the pendency of the appeal the respondent submitted a motion to remand. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
The respondent requests that his claim be remanded in order for the Immigration Judge to consider legal arguments regarding his eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). He contends that his previous counsel failed to raise established legal precedent, and further compounded his error by conceding that the respondent had been convicted of an aggravated felony, prejudicing the respondent’s claim in its entirety. He argues that the professional incompetence of the counsel representing him in Immigration Court deprived the respondent of due process. The respondent’s current counsel indicated in an affidavit that a complaint had been filed with the Illinois Attorney Registration and Disciplinary Commission against the respondent’s former counsel, and that a copy of the complaint had been supplied to the respondent’s former counsel.
 
Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, the Board has found that certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7th Cir. 2001) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada).
 
*2 While the respondent has indicated that he is attempting to comply with the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this matter arises, has held that, in addition to meeting the procedural requirements, an alien must also show prejudice. The Seventh Circuit held in Kay v. Ashcroft, 387 F.3d 664 (7th Cir. 2004) that an alien seeking to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must show that the alleged errors likely affected the outcome of the proceeding. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 558 (BIA 2003) (finding that the respondent had failed to show the requisite prejudiceto warrant consideration of his late appeal on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.) While the respondent argues that there was ineffective assistance of counsel because his previous counsel conceded that the respondent had been convicted of an aggravated felony, the respondent does not suggest that, had previous counsel not done so, the outcome would have been affected, particularly in light of the Immigration Judge’s analysis, and current legal precedent. Moreover, the respondent has not indicated what arguments he would have presented before the Immigration Judge that would have changed the outcome of the proceedings. Accordingly, as the respondent was not shown, by the previous counsel’s conduct, to have experienced actual prejudice during the course of the proceeding before the Immigration Judge, there can be no ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is without merit, and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
Turning to the underlying issues on appeal, the respondent is a lawful permanent resident of the United States. The record reflects that on or about November 14, 2003, he entered a plea of guilty in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, to a charge of possession of cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the Illinois Compiled Statutes (ILCS), for which he was sentenced to 12 months court supervision. Furthermore, on October 13, 2005, he was convicted in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, to a charge of possession of cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS; on November 10, 2005, he was convicted in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, to a charge of possession of cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS, and on January 27, 2006, he was convicted in the Circuit Court for the Sixteenth Judicial Circuit of Kane County, Illinois, to a charge of possession of cannabis in violation of section 550/4(a) of chapter 720 of the ILCS (Gp. Exh. 2). Based on these convictions, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was removable as an alien who has been convicted of aggravated felonies under section 101 (a)(43)(B) of the Act, rendering him statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(3).
 
*3 After reviewing the respondent’s appellate arguments, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. The Seventh Circuit has held that an alien’s state drug conviction, which is a felony under state law, but which only qualifies as a misdemeanor under federal law, is not an aggravated felony under section 101 (a)(43)(B) of the Act. See Gonzales-Gomez v. Achim, 441 F.3d 532 (7th Cir. 2006). However, the Seventh Circuit subsequenttly held, in Tunis v. Gonzales, 447 F.3d 547 (7th Cir. 2006), that a drug offense, state or federal, that would be punishable as a felony under federal law, is an aggravated felony for purposes of immigration law.
 
Under this precedent, the respondent’s 2005 and 2006 convictions plainly qualify as aggravated felonies because, with his 2003 conviction final, his subsequent convictions for possession of a controlled substance violate the Federal Controlled Substances Act (CSA), 21 U.S.C. § 802 et seq., and are punishable as felonies under the CSA. See 21 U.S.C. § 844. In short, the Immigration Judge’s legal conclusion in the respondent’s case is correct under applicable legal standards. The respondent is removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony, making him ineligible for cancellation of removal.1
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We are not aware of a Seventh Circuit ruling involving two simple possession convictions. But the respondent’s second possession offense was punishable as a felony had it been prosecuted, following federal procedural requirements, in federal court. While we do not know how a federal prosecutor would have prosecuted this offense, the respondent did commit a felony violation of the CSA. See Gonzales-Gomez v. Achim, supra, at 534-35.

2006 WL 3203685 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMIREZ-POSADA, ABEL DE JESUS
FILE: AXX-XX2-901 - LOS ANGELES
August 25, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
CARLOS A. CRUZ
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 31, 2006. The record reflects that the respondent was given, in court, the notice of the next hearing date and the consequences for a failure to appear. He failed to appear for his hearing on November 20, 1990. He was ordered to be deported in proceedings conducted in absentia.
 
More than 14 years later, he submitted a motion to reopen to apply for benefits under the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act (NACARA). He claimed that his notary advised him that he would be deported if he went to the hearing. He stated that his fear of deportation, limited education, and the false information from his notary establish reasonable cause and exceptional circumstances for reopening regardless of timeliness, citing Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I&N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, as well as his subsequent motion to reconsider. She distinguished Matter of Cruz-Garcia, supra, from the respondent’s case. She found that the respondent had not established ineffective assistance of counsel or non-attorney fraud so as to warrant reopening. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). She concluded that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely, his NACARA application was untimely and not supported by evidence that he is eligible for such relief, and he had not shown due diligence so as to warrant equitable tolling of the time limits for such motions.
 
On appeal, the respondent states that he is not claiming ineffective assistance. He asserts that his fear, his limited education, and his faith in the notary who deceived him amount to reasonable cause and exceptional circumstances to warrant reopening under Matter of Cruz-Garcia, supra, without regard to timeliness. We are persuaded that our decision in Matter of Cruz-Garcia, supra, is applicable here, and that the respondent’s motion is not untimely. The correct standard for reopening the respondent’s in absentia proceedings is “reasonable cause,” not “exceptional circumstances.” We find that the respondent has not demonstrated a reasonable cause for his failure to appear. Moreover, he has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief he seeks, and he has not shown diligence in these proceedings. We find no basis for reopening the respondent’s deportation proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
