2006 WL 3485670 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA EVA RODRIGUEZ DE MADRIGAL
FILE: AXX XX4 481 - SAN DIEGO
August 24, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board when the respondent appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings conducted in absentia. On November 29, 2005, we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal to support her allegations that she failed to appear at her scheduled hearing of May 10, 2005, on account of the ineffective assistance of her prior attorney. On remand, the Immigration Judge noted that the directives in the Board’s decision were subject to various interpretations. Nevertheless, the Immigration Judge also noted that the respondent’s evidence was insufficient to establish her burden of proof for reopening her proceedings. Therefore, the Immigration Judge certified the case back to the Board for clarification. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
As a preliminary matter, we clarify that our intent on remanding the record to the Immigration Judge was to consider the evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal to determine whether it adequately established that she received ineffective assistance of counsel, warranting rescission of the removal order entered against her in absentia. We did not intend to reopen the proceedings without proper consideration of the evidence by the Immigration Judge.
 
Consequently, in reviewing the Immigration Judge’s ultimate conclusion that the evidence does not establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that neither party submitted briefs in response to the Immigration Judge’s certification of this case. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(c) and 1003.7. The Immigration Judge determined that the letter submitted by respondent’s former counsel, John F. Lenderman, in support of the respondent’s appeal, raised several questions regarding possible collusion between the respondent and counsel in making an alleged claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge’ s decision has not been shown to be clearly erroneous. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). In addition, given the respondent’s failure to meet the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s conclusion in this case. See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3252576 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIN QUAN LIN
FILE: AXX XX1 917 - PHOENIX
August 22, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION/APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joy H. Huang, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sandra B, Myles
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 12, 2006, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his case following a hearing held on June 24, 1993, in which he was ordered deported in absentia, as untimely filed and failing to comply with the requirements for a claim based on ineffective assistance of counsel set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Pursuant to section 242B(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c), an order of deportation entered in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 242B(c)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to circumstances beyond the respondent’s control, such as a serious illness or the death of a member of the respondent’s immediate family. See, e.g., Mailer of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that his failure to appear for his scheduled hearing was due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, who he later discovered was not an attorney, and that therefore the in absentia order should be rescinded due to “exceptional circumstances.” Specifically, the respondent contends that this individual not only failed to inform him that he had a scheduled hearing which resulted in the order of deportation being entered in his absence, but also advised him that his case had been closed and that he did not need to appear for any hearings.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit wherein this case arises, has held that equitable tolling of a filing deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf, Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-99 (9th Cir. 2003); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1225 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1184 (9th Cir. 2001) (en banc); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999)(finding that statute of limitations for reopening an in absentia order of deportation is tolled as an equitable matter where the late filing of the motion is attributed to the deceptive actions of a notary posing as an attorney, but diligence must be shown). In the matter before us, we fail to find the respondent exercised diligence in filing his motion to reopen. His motion to reopen was filed more than 13 years after the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia deportation order. According to the respondent, a non-attorney told him “that his case was closed and that ¿heº did not need to appear in any court hearing any more.” The respondent contends that he did not learn of the Immigration Judge’ s order of deportation until he began the process to adjust his status to that of a lawful permanent resident, based on his approved employment-related immigrant visa petition, in 2001. The respondent paid a substantial bond in order to released from detention in 1993. He fails to reveal if he took any steps to have the money returned to him in the intervening 13 years. Only after he was detained based on the outstanding order of deportation did the respondent express an interest in what had transpired 13 years before. Consequently, we find the respondent did not exercise diligence in waiting more than 13 years to investigate his immigration status. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; Lopez v. INS, supra.
 
*2 Even if the respondent’s motion were to be accepted as having been timely filed with the Immigration Judge, it fails to even minimally assert a proper ineffective assistance claim. See Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018, 1020-21 (9th Cir. 2002) (examining the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in a claim of ineffective assistance by non-attorneys); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements may be sufficient in some circumstances).
 
The respondent alleges that he wrote a letter containing his accusations of ineffective assistance to the non-attorney and contacted the California State Bar Association. However, he presented no evidence in support of the allegations. Taking such a step would have provided the non-attorney with an opportunity to provide his own version of events. Alternatively, if the mailing was returned by the postal service as undeliverable, the returned mailing would have at least supported the respondent’s claim that he could not locate him. Moreover, the respondent has not presented a copy of a contract or agreement with this individual indicating what services were to be performed by him. We therefore find that the motion provides an inadequate basis from which to conclude that the non-attorney failed to provide services that he had agreed to provide which constituted ineffective assistance and prejudiced the respondent. See generally Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (an inquiry into whether counsel provided ineffective assistance must begin with determining whether competent counsel would have acted otherwise before examining whether an alien was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance).
 
In addition, we find the respondent’s claims as to ineffective assistance of counsel, did not relieve him of the responsibility of notifying the Immigration Court as to any address changes. The respondent was required to provide written notice of any change in his address to the Immigration Court within 5 working days of the actual change. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.15(c)(2) (1993). The Order to Show Cause explained this requirement, and the respondent was also provided oral notice of the same in his native language. Because the respondent failed to comply with his responsibility to advise the Immigration Court of any change in address, and as the notice of hearing was sent to the most recent address provided by the respondent (and not his attorney as he claims on appeal), we conclude that the Immigration Judge was correct to proceed in absentia, and we see no reason the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order should be rescinded. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.26(b)(2)(1993); see also 8 C.F.R. § 3.26(c)(1993) (noting that written notice to the most recent address contained in the Record of Proceeding shall be considered sufficient).
 
*3 Finally, even though the respondent demonstrated that he is eligible for a form of relief from deportation which was not available at his hearing in this case adjustment of status, the respondent’s situation does not fall within the purview of our decision in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). First, in Matter of M-S-, we held that an alien who fails to appear at his or her hearing and who had not received oral warnings of the consequences for a failure to appear, could have proceedings reopened in order to pursue a form of relief which was not available at the time of the hearing so long as the motion to reopen met the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.2(c) and 3.23(b) (1998). In this case, the certificate of service on the Order to Show Cause (form I-221) (Exh. 1) indicates that it was personally served upon the respondent and that a Service officer read the contents of the document to the respondent in Mandarin, his native language. The consequences for failing to appear at a hearing are listed on page 4 of the Order to Show Cause and therefore are part of the contents read to him. The respondent signed the form at the bottom of page 5 acknowledging that it was read and given to him. Thus, the respondent received oral notice of the consequences for failure to appear at a hearing.
 
Second, the respondent’s motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge was filed on May 2, 2006. This is a date well after the September 30, 1996, deadline for the submission of a motion to reopen proceedings in which the final administrative order from the Immigration Judge was dated June 24, 1993. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). As such, it is clearly untimely. Id.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal of the denial of his motion to reopen is dismissed.
